The Erroneous Statement and Its Confusion
Al-Taftazani quotes and critiques al-Tusi's confusing statement about quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific erroneous statement al-Tusi made in al-Tajrid and how it confuses two terminological systems.

Core Idea
Al-Tusi's statement in al-Tajrid mixes his own terminology with later conventions, creating confusion.

After accusing al-Tusi of writing something contradictory, al-Taftazani quotes the exact statement from al-Tajrid. This statement blurs the distinction between the conditioned and unconditioned quiddity and mixes two different terminological traditions. Al-Taftazani calls it obvious error and confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the quoted statement
Al-Tusi says the quiddity can be taken stripped of everything and is the quiddity with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā shay'), existing only in the mind, and also can be unconditioned (lā bi-sharṭ shay'), which is a natural universal existing externally and a part of individuals.
2
Identify the confusion
Al-Taftazani notes this mixes what al-Tusi himself said in his Commentary on al-Isharat with what later scholars popularized.
3
Understand the implication
This confusion provides evidence that al-Tajrid is not authentically al-Tusi's work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الحكيم المحقق (۱) [هو نصير الدين أبو جعفر محمد بن محمد الطوسي] مع مبالغته في أن المأخوذ بشرط أن يكون وحده هو الجزء (۲) [في (ب) الجوهر بدلاً من (الجزء)] الموجود في الخارج، وأن المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء هو المحمول، وليس بجزء أصلاً، وإنما يقال له جزء الماهية بالمجاز، لما أنه يشبه الجزء من جهة أن اللفظ الدال عليه يقع جزءاً من حدها، أورد هذا الكلام في كتاب التجريد (۱) [سبق التعريف بهذا الكتاب] على وجه يشهد بأنه ليس من تصانيفه وذلك أنه قال (۲) [سقط من (ب) لفظ (قال)] : قد تؤخذ الماهية محذوفاً عنها ما عداها بحيث لو انضم إليها شيء لكان زائداً عليها، ولا يكون هو (۳) [في (ب) هي بدلاً من (هو)] مقولاً على ذلك المجموع الحاصل منها، ومن الشيء المنضم إليها، والمأخوذ على هذا الوجه هو الماهية بشرط لا شيء، ولا توجد إلا في الأذهان وقد توجد الماهية لا بشرط شيء وهو كلي طبيعي موجود في الخارج، هو جزء من الأشخاص، وصادق على المجموع الحاصل منه، ومما انضاف إليه، وهذا خبط ظاهر وخلط لما ذكره في شرح الإشارات بما (٤) [في (ب) إنما بدلاً من (بما)] اشتهر بين المتأخرين وفيه شهادة صادقة بما رمى به ،التجريد من أنه ليس من تصانيفه جلالة قدره عن أن ينسب إلى غيره . مع
And know that the verifying philosopher [al-Tusi], despite his insistence that what is taken with the condition of being alone is the part existing in the external, and that what is taken unconditionally is the predicate and is not a part at all, but is called a part of the quiddity metaphorically because it resembles a part in that the word denoting it occurs as a part of its definition, he presented this statement in the book al-Tajrid in a manner that testifies that it is not from his works. For he said: The quiddity may be taken stripped of everything besides itself such that if something were joined to it, it would be additional to it, and it would not be predicated of that composite resulting from it and from the thing joined to it. That which is taken in this manner is the quiddity with the condition of nothing, and it does not exist except in minds. And the quiddity may exist unconditioned, and it is a natural universal existing in the external, it is a part of individuals, and it is true of the composite resulting from it and from what is added to it. And this is obvious error and confusion of what he mentioned in the Commentary on al-Isharat with what has become prevalent among later [scholars]. And in it there is a true testimony to the accusation that al-Tajrid is not from his works, [given] the loftiness of his rank from being attributed to others.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage contains a sharp criticism of a statement by al-Tusi (the 'verifying philosopher') in his book al-Tajrid. Al-Taftazani notes that despite al-Tusi's insistence that the part taken with the condition of being alone (bi-sharṭ an yakūna wahdahu) is the actual part existing in the external, and the unconditioned (lā bi-sharṭ shay') is the predicate and not a part at all but called a part of quiddity metaphorically, yet in al-Tajrid he wrote something that contradicts this. Al-Tusi said: 'The quiddity may be taken stripped of everything besides itself such that if something were joined to it, it would be additional to it, and it would not be predicated of the composite of it and that thing. That which is taken in this manner is the quiddity with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā shay'), and it does not exist except in minds. And the quiddity may exist unconditioned (lā bi-sharṭ shay'), which is a natural universal existing in the external, is a part of individuals, and is true of the composite of it and what is added to it.' Al-Taftazani calls this an obvious error and confusion, mixing what al-Tusi himself had stated in his commentary on al-Isharat with what has become known among later scholars. The passage ends by saying that this is a true testimony to the accusation that al-Tajrid is not from al-Tusi's own works, as he is too lofty to be attributed to others.

Key Terms
بشرط لا شيء (bi-sharṭ lā shay', conditioned by nothing)
لا بشرط شيء (lā bi-sharṭ shay', unconditioned)
كلي طبيعي (kullī ṭabī‘ī, natural universal)
جزء (juz', part)
ماهية (māhiyya, quiddity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the conditioned and unconditioned quiddity are clearly distinguished in al-Tusi's work.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that the quoted statement mixes terminology and creates confusion, indicating possible misattribution.

Checkpoint
What does al-Taftazani call al-Tusi's statement in al-Tajrid?
A) Clear and consistent.
B) Obvious error and confusion.
C) A new insight.
D) A quote from Avicenna.

Key Takeaway
The quoted statement from al-Tajrid mixes two terminological systems, leading al-Taftazani to question its authenticity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that you consider absolutely clear, how do you know that you haven't unknowingly mixed two different terminologies that obscure the truth? What would it take to examine your own assumptions with the same critical eye Taftazani uses here?
?
Al-Taftazani uses a single confused statement to question an entire book's authenticity. Is there any area of your religious knowledge where you rely on a source without examining its internal consistency? Will you now take the time to study it more carefully?
?
If even a scholar of al-Tusi's rank can be accused of confusion due to terminological shifts, how much more careful must you be when you speak about Allah and His attributes? Do you guard your words against mixing different intellectual traditions without realizing it?

Critique of the Confused View on Essence with Condition
A critique of a confused view on essence taken bishart la shay'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's criticism of a confused account of essence taken with the condition of nothing (bishart la shay') and why he argues it indicates misattribution of al-Tajrid.

Core Idea
Not every view on essence is coherent; this one is a muddle that reveals authorship doubts.

The author continues his refinement of key concepts by exposing a flawed understanding of essence taken with a condition. He quotes a passage from al-Tajrid that he argues is not only mistaken but also evidence that the book is not by its reputed author. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the shift from the author's own comment to a quotation from al-Tajrid.
The passage contains a direct quote; pay attention to where it begins.
2
Identify the two conceptions of essence
The quote from al-Tajrid presents two ways of taking essence: one with the condition of nothing (bishart la shay') and one without condition (la bishart shay'). Note how each is defined and where each exists.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author calls the quoted view 'apparent confusion and mixing'. Ask yourself: what exactly is being confused? The commentary will clarify that it conflates discussions from Sharh al-Isharat with later terminology.
4
Grasp the authorship implication
The author concludes that this confusion supports the claim that al-Tajrid is not by the original author. Consider why such a muddled view would be unworthy of a great scholar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس بجزء أصلاً، وإنما يقال له جزء الماهية بالمجاز، لما أنه يشبه الجزء من جهة أن اللفظ الدال عليه يقع جزءاً من حدها، أورد هذا الكلام في كتاب التجريد على وجه يشهد بأنه ليس من تصانيفه وذلك أنه قال : قد تؤخذ الماهية محذوفاً عنها ما عداها بحيث لو انضم إليها شيء لكان زائداً عليها، ولا يكون هو مقولاً على ذلك المجموع الحاصل منها، ومن الشيء المنضم إليها، والمأخوذ على هذا الوجه هو الماهية بشرط لا شيء، ولا توجد إلا في الأذهان وقد توجد الماهية لا بشرط شيء وهو كلي طبيعي موجود في الخارج، هو جزء من الأشخاص، وصادق على المجموع الحاصل منه، ومما انضاف إليه، وهذا خبط ظاهر وخلط لما ذكره في شرح الإشارات بما اشتهر بين المتأخرين وفيه شهادة صادقة بما رمى به التجريد من أنه ليس من تصانيفه جلالة قدره عن أن ينسب إلى غيره .
And it is not a part at all; rather it is called a part of the essence (mahiyya) metaphorically, because it resembles a part in that the term signifying it occurs as a part of its definition. This statement was cited in the book al-Tajrid in a manner that testifies that it is not among his compositions: for he said: 'Essence may be taken with everything other than it omitted, such that if something were joined to it, it would be additional to it, and it would not be predicated of that composite resulting from it and from the thing joined to it. And what is taken in this manner is the essence with the condition of nothing (bishart la shay'), and it does not exist except in the mind. And essence may exist without any condition (la bishart shay'), and it is a natural universal existing in the external world; it is a part of individuals and true of the composite resulting from it and from what is joined to it.' And this is apparent confusion and mixing of what he mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat with what became well-known among the later scholars, and in it is a true testimony to what was alleged about al-Tajrid — that it is not among his compositions — due to the eminence of his rank from being attributed to others.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a specific view about essence (mahiyya) presented in the book al-Tajrid. The author begins by stating that something is not a part of essence at all; rather it is called a part metaphorically because the term signifying it appears as a part of the definition. He then reports that this statement was cited in al-Tajrid in a way that suggests it is not the work of the same author (likely referring to the author of Sharh al-Isharat). The author quotes al-Tajrid: 'Essence may be taken stripped of everything else, so that if something were added it would be extra; such an essence is not predicated of the composite. This is essence with the condition of nothing (bishart la shay'), existing only in the mind. Alternatively, essence without any condition (la bishart shay') is a natural universal existing externally, being a part of individuals and true of the composite.' The author then condemns this as 'apparent confusion and mixing' because it conflates the discussion from Sharh al-Isharat with later developments. He concludes that this confused passage actually supports the claim that al-Tajrid is not by the original author, whose stature is above such attribution to others.

Key Terms
ماهية (mahiyya)
جزء (juz')
مجاز (majaz)
بشرط لا شيء (bishart la shay')
كلي طبيعي (kulli tabi'i)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is rejecting any use of 'essence with a condition' entirely.
Correction: He only rejects the particular confused account in al-Tajrid that conflates different meanings; he does not deny that essence can be taken with conditions.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the passage from al-Tajrid regarding essence?
A) It correctly defines essence with a condition.
B) It is a clear and precise explanation.
C) It is confused and supports the claim that al-Tajrid is not by its attributed author.
D) It is identical to the view in Sharh al-Isharat.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that a confused account of essence taken bishart la shay' reveals that al-Tajrid is not authentically by its attributed author.

The author uses a specific error to question the authenticity of an entire text. In your own spiritual journey, where might a small confusion be leading you to accept teachings that may not be sound? Will you investigate their origins today?

Simple and Composite Essence: Definitions and the Fallacy of Necessity
Sanusi defines simple/composite essence and begins critiquing the proof of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of simple and composite essence, the two arguments for the necessity of simple essence, and why the first argument is rejected as a fallacy.

Core Idea
Composite essence does not necessarily prove simple essence—the first argument commits a fallacy.

This passage from the Third Investigation introduces a fundamental distinction: essence is either simple (having no parts) or composite (having parts). The author presents two arguments that composite essence must terminate in simple essence, but he immediately weakens them. Learning to spot the fallacy in the first argument sharpens your kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the examples of simple essence (the Necessary, point, unit, existence) and composite essence (body, man, blackness).
Simple essence has no parts; composite essence has parts.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage gives two arguments: (1) every number, even infinite, contains a unit, so composite implies simple; (2) in intellectual composites, an infinite regress would preclude understanding.
The first argument is absolute; the second is specific to intellectual composition.
3
Understand the critique
The author calls the first argument a fallacy (mugalata) of confusing the subject (ma'ruḍ) with the attribute ('ariḍ). The unit belongs to the number, not to what is numbered.
The critique continues beyond the passage, but the error is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث: الماهية بسيطة ومركبة قال : المبحث الثالث: الضرورة قاضية بوجود الماهية المركبة فلا بد من انتهائها إلى البسيطة) الماهية : إما بسيطة لا جزء لها أصلاً كالواجب والنقطة والوحدة والوجود وإما مركبة لها أجزاء كالجسم والإنسان والسواد ووجود المركبة معلوم بالضرورة، ويلزم منه وجود البسيطة، إما مطلقاً فلأن كل عدد ولو غير متناه ، فالواحد موجود فيه بالضرورة، وإما في المركب العقلي، فلأنه لو لم ينته إلى البسيط امتنع تعقل الماهية لامتناع إحاطة العقل بما لا يتناهى، وكلاهما ضعيف . أما الأول : فلأنه مغالطة من باب اشتباه المعروض بالعارض، فإن وجود ما لا الواحد بمعنى جزء له أصلا، إنما يلزم في العدد الذي هو العارض وإما في معروض العدد فلا يلزم إلا معروض الواحد الذي هو أحد أجزائه، فعلى
The third investigation: Essence is simple and composite. He said: The third investigation: Necessity judges the existence of composite essence, so it must terminate in the simple. Essence is either simple, having no part at all, like the Necessary, the point, the unit, and existence; or composite, having parts, like body, man, and blackness. The existence of the composite is known by necessity, and from it follows the existence of the simple. Either absolutely, because every number, even infinite, necessarily has a unit in it; or in the intellectual composite, because if it did not terminate in the simple, the intellection of essence would be impossible, due to the impossibility of the mind encompassing what is infinite. And both are weak. As for the first: it is a fallacy (mugalata) of the type of confusing the subject with the attribute; for the existence of what does not have a unit in the sense of a part at all follows only in the number which is the attribute; but in the subject of the number, it follows only the subject of the unit which is one of its parts. So on...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage presents the Third Investigation (al-mabhas al-thalith) concerning simple and composite essence. It begins by stating that necessity judges the existence of composite essence, and therefore it must ultimately terminate in a simple essence. The passage then defines: essence is either simple (basiṭa) without any part, like the Necessary Existent (al-wajib), a point, the unit, and existence; or composite (murakkaba) having parts, like body, man, and blackness. The existence of the composite is known by necessity, and from this the existence of the simple is inferred. Two arguments are given: (1) Absolutely (muṭlaqan): every number, even an infinite one, necessarily contains a unit; thus composite numbers imply a simple unit. (2) In the intellectual composite (al-murakkab al-'aqli): if the chain of intellectual parts did not terminate in a simple, the mind could not grasp the essence, since it cannot encompass an infinite regress. The author then states that both arguments are weak (ḍa'if). He begins to critique the first: it is a fallacy (mugalata) of confusing the subject (ma'ruḍ) with the attribute ('ariḍ). The existence of what does not have a unit as a part at all applies only to the attribute (the number itself), not to the subject of that number. For the subject (the numbered thing), it follows only that it has a unit as one of its parts, not that it is a simple unit. The passage cuts off at 'fa-'ala' (so on...), indicating the critique continues on the next page.

Key Terms
ماهية (mahiyya)
بسيطة (basiṭa)
مركبة (murakkaba)
عدد ('adad)
واحد (wahid)
مغالطة (mugalata)
معروض (ma'ruḍ)
عارض ('ariḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author endorses the argument that composite essence necessarily proves simple essence.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states both arguments are weak and begins explaining why the first is a fallacy.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the first argument for the necessity of simple essence weak?
A) Because numbers cannot be infinite.
B) Because it confuses the subject with the attribute.
C) Because the unit is not a part of number.
D) Because composite essence does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that the inference from composite to simple essence is not logically necessary; the first argument commits a fallacy of confusing the subject with the attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take a moment to recall one such argument and honestly ask: did I fully understand the reasoning, or was I just impressed by the conclusion? What will you do differently next time you hear a 'proof' about Allah's essence?

Are you ready to admit when your own reasoning about religious truths is flawed, even if it means letting go of a comfortable belief? What is one belief about Allah or faith that you hold firmly, and are you willing to subject it to the same scrutiny Sanusi applied here?
?
The fallacy of confusing the subject with the attribute is subtle—it can feel correct while being wrong. In your daily life, do you ever confuse your own perceptions or feelings (attributes) with the actual nature of things (subjects)? How might this lead you to misunderstand Allah's attributes, such as mistaking a human conception of mercy for the reality of Allah's mercy?

Simplicity and Composition as Correlatives
Sanusi distinguishes absolute vs. correlative simplicity and composition, and refutes a false claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how simplicity and composition can be considered as relative terms (mutadayifan) and why a common claim about their specificity is false.

Core Idea
Simplicity and composition shift meaning between absolute contradiction and relative correlation.

Sanusi continues his analysis of key kalam terms by exploring the relationship between simplicity (basata) and composition (tarkib) when they are treated as correlatives. He first describes the logical relationships that arise between simple things and composite things under this relative consideration, then rejects an erroneous claim about how the real simple relates to the relative simple. This passage sharpens our understanding of how the same term can function differently in absolute vs. relative contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage has a dense logical structure; read slowly.
2
Identify the two relational frameworks
The author first describes the relationships when simplicity and composition are considered as correlatives, noting two subcases: with and without a condition on the relation. Then he refutes another claim and finally explains the absolute sense as contradictories.
The key is to distinguish when they are taken as relative vs. absolute.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author rejects the view that the real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative simple, and that the composite is the opposite. Understand why this is false based on the definitions.
This refutation is central to the lesson.
4
Contrast absolute and relative meanings
Note that when taken absolutely (as earlier defined), simplicity and composition are contradictory attributes. But they can also be taken correlatively, where the simple is defined in relation to what it composes.
This duality is a subtle but important point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تعتبر التركب والبساطة متضايفين فيكون بين البسيطين عموم من وجه وبين المركبين مساواة إن لم يشترط في الإضافة في اعتبار الإضافة وعموم مطلقاً إن اشترط. وما قيل : إن البسيط الحقيقي أخص مطلقاً من الإضافي والمركب بالعكس فاسد. البساطة والتركيب بالتفسير السابق وصفان متنافيان لا يصدقان على أصل، ولا يرتفعان لكونهما في قوة النقيضين، وقد يؤخذان متضايفين، بأن يؤخذ البسيط بسيطاً، بالقياس إلى ما تركب منه، بمعنى كونه شيئا
And composition and simplicity may be considered as correlatives, so between the two simple things there is generality in some respect, and between the two composite things equality if no condition is stipulated in the relation in consideration of relation, and absolute generality if a condition is stipulated. And what is said: that the real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative, and the composite is the opposite, is false. Simplicity and composition, according to the previous interpretation, are two contradictory attributes that do not apply to a thing, nor are they removed, because they are in the power of contradictories. And they may be taken as correlatives, such that the simple is taken as simple in relation to that from which it is composed, meaning that it is a thing.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage explores the relationship between simplicity (basata) and composition (tarkib) when considered as relative terms (mutadayifan). The author first states that when they are taken as correlatives, the relationship between two simple things is 'generality in some respect' (عموم من وجه) — meaning each is both more general and more specific in different aspects. Between two composite things, there is equality if no condition is imposed on the relation, but absolute generality if a condition is stipulated. 

Then the author rejects a claim: that the real simple (al-basit al-haqiqi) is absolutely more specific than the relative simple, and the composite is the opposite (i.e., the relative composite is absolutely more specific than the real composite). He declares this false. 

He then reiterates that simplicity and composition, according to the earlier definition (as non-relative, i.e., absolute), are two contradictory attributes that cannot both be true of a thing and cannot both be absent (like contradictories). However, they can also be taken as correlatives, where the simple is considered simple only relative to what it is composed of, meaning it is a thing that serves as a component. This distinction clarifies that concepts like simplicity and composition can have both absolute and relative meanings, and one must not conflate them.

Key Terms
بساطة (basata)
تركيب (tarkib)
متضايفين (mutadayifan)
حقيقي (haqiqi)
إضافي (idafi)
عموم من وجه (umum min wajh)
مساواة (musawa)
نقيضان (naqidan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative simple, and the composite is the opposite.
Correction: This claim is false (fasid) because the relationship between real and relative depends on context; they are not universally nested in that way.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the claim that the real simple (al-basit al-haqiqi) is absolutely more specific than the relative simple?
A) He affirms it as true.
B) He rejects it as false.
C) He says it is true only if consideration of relation is included.
D) He says it is a matter of definition.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of simplicity and composition shifts between absolute contradiction and relative correlation; the claim that the real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative simple is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's simplicity, do you ever slip into thinking of it as a relative simplicity—like God is simple only compared to creation? How does this passage's distinction between absolute and relative challenge your mental image of God? Will you correct this today?

Do you sometimes label someone as 'simple' or 'composite' in their understanding without considering the respect in which they are speaking? Will you become more charitable in your judgments starting now?

Objection and Answer on Necessary Composition
Author answers objection about necessary composition of shared essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when sharing an essential attribute leads to necessary composition and when it does not, and the role of accidental differences.

Core Idea
Shared essences do not always necessitate composition; accidents can be external.

This lesson addresses a key metaphysical objection: if two things share an essential attribute, must they both be composite? The author carefully distinguishes between different kinds of sharing and demonstrates that composition is not always required. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how simple essences can coexist.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the initial objection that everything with a part is composite, and consider its implied claim about shared essentials.
The objector assumes that if two quiddities share an essential, each must be composed of that essential plus a differentia.
2
Analyze the first part of the answer
Understand the scenario where the shared essential is the complete quiddity of one entity and a part of the other. Recognize that only one is composite.
Example: substance (جوهر) and body (جسم) — body's essential distinguishes it, but substance remains simple.
3
Examine the scenario of both composite
Consider cases like man and horse where both share an essential (animal) but each has a different differentia, making both composite.
This contrasts with the previous scenario.
4
Understand the key exception
Study the case where sharing is only in the essential while differences are in accidents. The author argues this does not entail composition because accidents can arise from external causes.
Example: unity (الوحدة) and point (النقطة) are simple yet share accidents like accidentally.
5
Reflect on the examples
Consider how the examples clarify the logical distinctions and why the objection fails universally.
This reinforces the correct understanding of composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمنزلة أن يقال : كل ما له جزء فهو مركب مع الاستغناء عن باقي المقدمات، وإن أريد ما ليس بعرضي جاز أن يكون الذاتي المشترك، تمام إحدى الماهيتين، وجزء الأخرى الممتازة عنها بالذاتي الآخر، أو بلوازم الماهية، فلا يلزم تركب الماهيتين جميعاً، كالجوهر مع الجسم المتميز عنه بالذاتي، ولوازم الماهية الجسمية . قلنا : المراد لزوم تركب الماهية الممتازة بالذاتي أو بلوازم الماهية، فإن كانت كلتاهما كذلك كما في الإنسان والفرس، فكلتاهما، وإن كانت إحداهما كما ذكرتم فإحداهما، وأما مجرد الاشتراك في ذاتي مع الاختلاف في العوارض الثبوتية أو السلبية، أو مجرد الاختلاف بالذاتي مع الاشتراك في العوارض، فلا يستلزم التركيب لجواز أن يكون الذاتي المشترك تمام ماهيتهما، ويستند اختلاف العوارض إلى أسباب غير الماهية كما في الإنسان وأفراده، وأن يكون الذاتيات المختلفات تمام الماهيتين البسيطتين المشتركتين في العوارض كالوحدة، والنقطة في العرضية والإمكان ونحو ذلك .
It is as if it were said: Everything that has a part is composite, dispensing with the remaining premises. And if what is not accidental is intended, it is possible that the shared essential is the complete [essence] of one of the two quiddities, and a part of the other distinguished from it by the other essential, or by concomitants of the quiddity, so composition of both quiddities is not necessary, like substance with body distinguished from it by the essential, and the concomitants of corporeal quiddity. We say: What is meant is the necessity of composition of the quiddity distinguished by the essential or by concomitants of the quiddity. If both are like that as in man and horse, then both; and if one of them is as you mentioned, then one. As for mere sharing in an essential with difference in positive or negative accidents, or mere difference by the essential with sharing in accidents, it does not necessitate composition because it is possible that the shared essential is the complete quiddity of both, and the difference of accidents is attributed to causes other than the quiddity as in man and his individuals, and that the different essentials are the complete quiddities of the two simple quiddities that share in accidents like unity, and the point in accidentally and contingency and the like.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the necessary composition of essences that share an essential attribute. The objector claims that if two quiddities share an essential, one must be composed of that essential plus another differentia. The author responds by distinguishing between cases: if the shared essential is the complete quiddity of one and a part of the other, only one is composite; if both are composites like man and horse, both are. Furthermore, mere sharing in essential with difference in accidents does not entail composition, because the accidents may derive from external causes. The author illustrates with examples: substance (جوهر) and body (جسم) where body's essential distinguishes it, yet substance remains simple; and unity (الوحدة) and point (النقطة) which are simple but share accidents like accidentally. The argument preserves the possibility of simple essences.

Key Terms
ذاتي مشترك
ماهية
تركيب
عوارض
جوهر
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That sharing any essential attribute forces both essences to be composite.
Correction: Sharing an essential does not necessarily force composition. If the shared essential is the complete quiddity of both, and differences are only in accidents arising from external causes, composition does not occur.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, when does sharing an essential not lead to composition?
A) When the shared essential is a part of both quiddities
B) When the shared essential is the complete quiddity of both and differences are in accidents only
C) When the two quiddities are entirely different in essence
D) When the quiddities are both accidental

Key Takeaway
Sharing an essential does not entail composition if the shared essential is the complete essence of both entities and their differences stem from accidental causes.

Simplicity and Composition as Correlatives
The author defines simplicity and composition as relative terms and refutes a previous claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how simplicity and composition function as correlative terms and the logical relationships between their real and relative types.

Core Idea
Simplicity and composition are relative terms that define each other.

Sanusi now introduces a new way of looking at simplicity and composition: as correlatives (متضايفين) that are defined relative to each other. This shift allows him to clarify the logical relationships between the various types of simple and composite entities, and to correct a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then read the literal translation. Note the key terms: بسيط حقيقي, بسيط إضافي, مركب حقيقي, مركب إضافي.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the logical relations
Focus on the phrases 'عموم من وجه' (generality in some respect), 'مساواة' (equality), and 'عموم مطلق' (absolute generality). Understand which pairs they apply to and under what conditions.
The condition is whether 'الإضافة' (relation) is stipulated in the consideration.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi rejects the statement that the real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative simple. Ask yourself: why would someone think that, and why is it wrong?
This connects to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد تعتبر التركب والبساطة متضايفين فيكون بين البسيطين عموم من وجه وبين المركبين مساواة إن لم يشترط في الإضافة في اعتبار الإضافة وعموم مطلقاً إن اشترط . وما قيل : إن البسيط الحقيقي أخص مطلقاً من الإضافي والمركب بالعكس فاسد .
He said: And composition and simplicity may be considered as correlatives, so between the two simples there is generality in some respect, and between the two composites there is equality if condition is not stipulated in the relation in considering the relation, and absolute generality if it is stipulated. And what is said: That the real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative simple, and the composite is the opposite, is invalid.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage introduces a new consideration: taking composition and simplicity as correlative terms (متضايفين). The author explains that when simplicity is considered relative to composition (i.e., something is simple in relation to what it composes), and composition relative to its parts, then the logical relations between 'real simple' (بسيط حقيقي) and 'relative simple' (بسيط إضافي) is 'general in some respect' (عموم من وجه). Between real composite and relative composite, if no condition is placed on the relation, they are equal (مساواة); but if consideration of the relation is stipulated, then absolute generality holds. The author then rejects a previously held view that the real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative simple, and the opposite for composites, declaring it invalid.

Key Terms
تركب
بساطة
متضايفين
بسيط حقيقي
بسيط إضافي
مركب حقيقي
مركب إضافي
عموم من وجه
مساواة
عموم مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative simple, i.e., every real simple is a relative simple, but not every relative simple is a real simple.
Correction: The relationship is actually 'generality in some respect' (عموم من وجه), meaning they overlap partially but neither is entirely contained within the other.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between real simple and relative simple according to the author?
A) Equality
B) Absolute generality of real simple over relative simple
C) Generality in some respect (عموم من وجه)
D) Real simple is always more specific

Key Takeaway
When simplicity and composition are considered as correlatives, the real simple and relative simple have a partial overlap, while the composites have equality or absolute generality depending on conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your understanding of Allah's oneness (tawhid), do you tend to think of it as a simple 'real simple' concept, ignoring that our human concepts of simplicity and complexity are relative? How might this lesson refine your awareness of Allah's absolute transcendence beyond such categories?
?
The passage rejects a simplistic hierarchy (real simple always more specific than relative). Where in your own aqeedah have you accepted a claim without examining its logical basis? Will you commit to re-examining one such belief today?

Logical Relations of Real and Relative Simple and Composite
The author elaborates on the logical relationships between real and relative simple/composite, including a critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced logical relationships between real and relative simplicity and composition, including the author's critique of a view from al-Tajrid.

Core Idea
Simplicity and composition can be contradictory or correlative, and their logical relations are not straightforward.

This passage dives into the logical intricacies of simplicity and composition as they apply to things in reality. It establishes that these terms can be understood in absolute or relative senses, and the relationships between them vary depending on how they are defined. The author also examines and critiques a specific position from al-Tajrid's work.

Guided Path
1
Understand the contradictory foundation
Read the first part where the author states that simplicity and composition, as absolute attributes, are contradictory and cannot coexist. Notice how they are like 'two contradictories'.
This sets the base for the correlative interpretation.
2
Trace the logical relations
Follow the author's analysis of the relationships: between real simple and relative simple (generality in some respect), and between real composite and relative composite (equality or absolute generality). Draw diagrams if helpful.
Pay attention to the shift from unconditional to conditional definitions.
3
Examine the critique of al-Tajrid
Read the author's presentation of the view from al-Tajrid and then his critique. Note the counterexample about the real simple that is not a part of anything.
This shows how careful one must be with logical universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البساطة والتركيب بالتفسير السابق وصفان متنافيان لا يصدقان على أصلا، ولا يرتفعان لكونهما في قوة النقيضين، وقد يؤخذان متضايفين، بأن يؤخذ البسيط بسيطاً، بالقياس إلى ما تركب منه، بمعنى كونه جزءاً منه والمركب مركباً بالقياس إلى جزئه بمعنى كونه كلا له، وهذا المعنى غير معنى كونه ذا جزء في الجملة وهو معنى المركب الحقيقي، وإن كان في نفسه من قبيل الإضافة، وبين البسيط الحقيقي والبسيط الإضافي عموم من وجه لتصادقهما في بسيط حقيقي هو جزء من مركب كالوحدة للعدد، وصدق الحقيقي بدون الإضافي في بسيط حقيقي لا يتركب منه شيء، كالواجب وبالعكس في مركب وقع جزءاً المركب كالجسم للحيوان وبين المركب الحقيقي والإضافي مساواة إن لم يشترط في الإضافي اعتبار الإضافة لأن كل مركب حقيقي، فهو مركب بالقياس إلى جزئه، وبالعكس وعموم مطلقاً إن اشترط ذلك، لأن مركب بالقياس إلى جزئه فهو مركب حقيقي، ولا نعكس لجواز أن لا تعتبر في الحقيقة الاضافة إلى جزئه فيكون أعم مطلقاً من الإضافي، وذكر في التجريد، أن البسيط الحقيقي أخص مطلقاً من الإضافي والمركب بالعكس . أما الأول : فلأن كل بسيط حقيقي فهو بسيط بالقياس إلى المركب منه، ولا ينعكس لجواز أن يكون البسيط الإضافي مركباً حقيقياً، كالجسم للحيوان والجدار للبيت. وأما الثاني: فلأن كل مركب إضافي مركب حقيقي، وليس كل مركب حقيقي مركباً إضافياً لجواز أن لا يعتبر فيه الإضافة، وفيه نظر، لأن البسيط الحقيقي، قد لا يكون بسيطاً إضافياً، بأن لا يعتبر جزءاً من شيء أصلاً. فالقول بأن المركب الحقيقي قد لا يكون إضافياً مع أن له جزءاً البتة،
Simplicity and composition according to the previous interpretation are two contradictory attributes that are not both true of a thing at all, and they cannot both be absent because they are like contradictories. And they may be taken as correlatives: by taking the simple as simple in relation to what it is composed of, meaning that it is a part of it, and the composite as composite in relation to its part, meaning that it is a whole of it. And this meaning is different from the meaning of 'having a part in general', which is the meaning of real composite, even if it itself is a kind of relation. And between real simple and relative simple there is generality in some respect because they both apply to a real simple that is a part of a composite, like unity for number; and real simple applies without relative simple in a real simple from which nothing is composed, like the Necessary; and the opposite in a composite that occurs as part of a composite, like body for animal. And between real composite and relative composite there is equality if condition is not stipulated in the relative considering the relation, because every real composite is composite in relation to its part, and vice versa; and absolute generality if that is stipulated, because [everything that is] composite in relation to its part is a real composite, but the converse is not necessary because it is possible that in reality the relation to its part is not considered, so it is absolutely more general than the relative. And it is mentioned in al-Tajrīd that the real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative simple, and the composite is the opposite. As for the first: because every real simple is simple in relation to what it is composed from, but the converse is not necessary because it is possible that a relative simple is a real composite, like body for animal and wall for house. And as for the second: because every relative composite is a real composite, but not every real composite is a relative composite because it is possible that the addition is not considered in it. But there is a consideration: because the real simple may not be a relative simple, in that it is not considered a part of anything at all. So the statement that the real composite may not be relative even though it certainly has a part...
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the logical relationships between real and relative simplicity and composition. The author first notes that simplicity and composition, as absolute attributes, are contradictory: a thing cannot be both simple and composite simultaneously, and they are like contradictories. However, they can be taken as correlatives, with different definitions: a simple relative to a composite is a part of it, and a composite relative to its part is a whole. This relative sense differs from the real sense of 'having a part'. The author then explains the logical relations: (1) Between real simple and relative simple: generality in some respect (عموم من وجه)—they both apply to a real simple that is a part of a composite (e.g., unity as a part of number), but a real simple may not be relative if it is not part of anything (e.g., the Necessary), and a relative simple may not be real if it is actually a composite (e.g., body relative to animal). (2) Between real composite and relative composite: if no condition is placed, they are equal (every real composite is relative to its part, and vice versa); but if the condition of considering the relation is stipulated, then relative composite is absolutely more special (because every relative composite is real, but not every real composite is considered relative if the relation is not considered). The author then mentions a view from al-Tajrīd that the real simple is absolutely more special than the relative simple, and the opposite for composites. The author provides the reasoning for that view but also critiques it: the real simple may not be a relative simple at all if it is not a part of anything, and the claim that a real composite may not be relative even though it has a part is flawed. The commentary clarifies the nuanced logical distinctions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All simple things are necessarily parts of composites, and all composites are considered relative to their parts in the same way.
Correction: The author shows that a real simple may not be a relative simple (e.g., the Necessary), and a real composite may not be relative if the relation is not considered.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between real composite and relative composite if the condition of considering the relation is stipulated?
A) Equality
B) Absolute generality of real composite over relative composite
C) Generality in some respect
D) Relative composite is absolutely more special

Key Takeaway
The logical relationships between real and relative simplicity and composition are nuanced, with different relations depending on definitions, and the author critiques a universal claim from al-Tajrid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to seek truth, but do you carefully distinguish between what is simple and what is composite in your own beliefs? Are you willing to admit when something you thought was simple (like your own self) is actually composed of many parts?
?
The Necessary is described as a real simple not part of anything. Does your understanding of God's oneness reflect this absolute simplicity, or do you unconsciously attribute parts to Him? How will you guard your heart from associating any composition with the Creator?
?
The author critiques a view from al-Tajrid. When you encounter disagreements among scholars, do you examine the logical foundations yourself, or do you merely follow one side? Will you commit to thinking carefully about arguments rather than just memorizing conclusions?

Real and Relative Simple and Composite: Definitions and Distinction
Defining real and relative simple and composite with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the real and relative senses of 'simple' and 'composite' and how they are defined in relation to parts.

Core Idea
Simple and composite have two meanings: one based on having parts, the other on being a whole for a part.

Sanusi begins by clarifying that the terms 'simple' and 'composite' are not univocal. They can be understood in a real sense (having no parts or having parts) or in a relative sense (being a part of something else or being a whole containing parts). This lesson unpacks these four meanings and lays the foundation for analyzing their logical relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation focusing on how the author defines 'composite' in two ways: relative (idaafi) and real (haqiiqi). Note the same applies to 'simple'.
2
Identify the four terms
Identify the four technical terms: real simple, relative simple, real composite, relative composite. Understand how they differ.
Real simple has no parts; real composite has parts. Relative simple is a part of something; relative composite is a whole containing parts.
3
See the example
Look at the example of unity and number: unity is a real simple (has no parts) and also a relative simple (it is a part of number). This illustrates how one thing can be both.
Not all real simples are relative simples, as with the Necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزءاً منه والمركب مركباً بالقياس إلى جزئه بمعنى كونه كلا له، وهذا المعنى غير معنى كونه ذا جزء في الجملة وهو معنى المركب الحقيقي، وإن كان في نفسه من قبيل الإضافة، وبين البسيط الحقيقي والبسيط الإضافي عموم من وجه لتصادقهما في بسيط حقيقي هو جزء من مركب (۱) . كالوحدة للعدد (۲) ، وصدق الحقيقي بدون الإضافي في بسيط حقيقي لا يتركب منه شيء، كالواجب وبالعكس في مركب وقع جزءا المركب كالجسم للحيوان وبين المركب الحقيقي والإضافي مساواة إن (۳) لم يشترط في الإضافي اعتبار الإضافة لأن كل مركب حقيقي ، فهو مركب بالقياس إلى جزئه، وبالعكس وعموم مطلقاً إن اشترط ذلك ) ، لأن مركب بالقياس إلى جزئة فهو مركب حقيقي، ولا نعكس لجواز أن لا تعتبر في الحقيقة الاضافة إلى جزئه فيكون أعم مطلقاً من الإضافي، وذكر في التجريد، أن البسيط الحقيقي أخص مطلقاً من الإضافي (0) أما الأول : فلأن كل بسيط حقيقي فهو بسيط بالقياس إلى المركب منه، ولا ينعكس لجواز أن يكون البسيط الإضافي مركباً حقيقياً، كالجسم للحيوان والجدار للبيت. وأما الثاني: فلأن كل مركب إضافي مركب حقيقي، وليس كل مركب حقيقي مركباً إضافياً لجواز أن لا يعتبر فيه الإضافة، وفيه نظر، لأن البسيط الحقيقي، قد لا يكون بسيطاً إضافياً، بأن لا يعتبر جزءاً من شيء أصلاً) . فالقول بأن المركب الحقيقي قد لا يكون إضافياً مع أن له جزءاً البتة، (۱) في (ب) جزء من (مركبات). (۲) سقط من (ب) لفظ (العدد) . (۳) سقط من (أ) لفظ (ان) . : (٤) في (ب) (وكل مركب باسقاط (الآن). (٥) في (أ) الحقيقي بدلاً من الإضافي). (٦) سقط من (أ) كلمة (أصلاً) . والبسيط الحقيقي يكون إضافياً البتة، أنه لا يلزم أن يكون جزءاً من شيء أصلاً فضلاً عن اعتبار ذلك باطل قطعاً .
... of it, and the composite is composite in relation to its part in the sense of being a whole for it, and this meaning is different from the meaning of its having a part in general, which is the meaning of real composite, although in itself it is of the category of relation. And between the real simple and the relative simple there is general/specific in some respects, because they coincide in a real simple that is a part of a composite (1), like unity for number (2), and the real applies without the relative in a real simple from which nothing is composed, like the Necessary, and conversely in a composite that occurs as part of a composite, like body for animal. And between the real composite and the relative composite there is equality if (3) the consideration of relation is not stipulated in the relative, because every real composite is a composite in relation to its part, and conversely. And absolute generality if that is stipulated, because [a thing] composite in relation to its part is a real composite, but not conversely, because it is possible that in reality the relation to its part is not considered, so it would be absolutely more general than the relative. And it is mentioned in al-Tajrid that the real simple is absolutely more specific than the relative. As for the first: because every real simple is simple in relation to the composite from it, but not conversely, because the relative simple may be a real composite, like body for animal and wall for house. And as for the second: because every relative composite is a real composite, but not every real composite is a relative composite, because it is possible that relation is not considered in it. And there is a critical view: because the real simple may not be a relative simple, in that it is not considered a part of anything at all. So the statement that the real composite may not be relative while it definitely has a part, and the real simple is definitely relative, [shows] that it does not necessarily follow that it is a part of anything at all, let alone that such consideration is absolutely false.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a classification and analysis of the logical relationships between the terms 'simple' and 'composite' when taken in their real (haqiiqi) sense versus their relative (idaafi) sense. The author first distinguishes the two meanings of composite: a thing is called composite in relation to its part (relative composite) and also in the sense of having a part (real composite). The same distinction applies to simple. Then he explores the logical relations (general/specific, equality, etc.) between these four categories.

He begins: a thing is called composite in relation to its part, meaning it is a whole for that part; this is different from simply having a part (real composite). The real composite is itself a relation but a specific one. Then he states that between real simple (basiit haqiiqi) and relative simple (basiit idaafi) there is a relationship of 'general/specific in some respects' (umoom min wajh): they intersect in cases where a real simple is also a part of a composite, like unity being a part of number; but they diverge where a real simple is not part of anything (like the Necessary) and where a relative simple is itself a real composite (like body, which is simple relative to animal but composite in itself).

Next, he examines the relationship between real composite (murakkab haqiiqi) and relative composite (murakkab idaafi). If being relative does not require considering the relation to a part, then they are coextensive (musaawa). If it does require that consideration, then the relative composite is more specific than the real composite, because every relative composite is a real composite, but not every real composite is relative (since one might not consider its relation to its part).

He then cites al-Tajrid, which states that real simple is absolutely more specific than relative simple. He provides two arguments: first, every real simple is simple relative to its composite, but not conversely, because a relative simple may be a real composite (like body). Second, every relative composite is a real composite, but not every real composite is relative. However, he notes a problem: a real simple may not be a relative simple if it is not part of anything. The claim that a real composite may not be relative (despite having a part) and a real simple is always relative is criticized: it does not follow that a real simple must be a part of something; in fact, it can be entirely independent, so the claim is false. The discussion includes textual variants from different manuscripts (noted in parentheses).

Key Terms
بسيط حقيقي
بسيط إضافي
مركب حقيقي
مركب إضافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between real simple and relative simple when the real simple is not part of anything?
A) They are coextensive.
B) The real simple is absolutely more specific.
C) The real simple applies without the relative simple.
D) They have a general/specific relationship in some respects.

Key Takeaway
Simple and composite each have a real meaning (based on having/not having parts) and a relative meaning (based on being a part or a whole), and these meanings are not always coextensive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the oneness of God, do you truly understand it as a real simplicity that admits no parts or composition? What changes in your worship if you confuse real simplicity with relative simplicity?
?
You are a composite of body and soul in the real sense. In your daily life, do you act as if your physical body is your entire self, ignoring your relative simplicity as a whole with parts? How would acknowledging your real composite nature reshape your priorities?

Logical Relationships Between Real and Relative Categories
Analyzing relationships: musaawa, a'amm mutlaqan, and critique of al-Tajrid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationships (equality, absolute generality, absolute specificity) between real and relative simple and composite, including a critique of a claim from al-Tajrid.

Core Idea
The relationships between real and relative terms are governed by whether relation is considered.

Having defined the four terms, Sanusi now analyzes the logical relations that hold between them. He examines the conditions under which real and relative composite are coextensive or one is more general, and similarly for simple. He also engages with a view from al-Tajrid that real simple is absolutely more specific than relative simple, offering a critical perspective.

Guided Path
1
Trace the two scenarios for composite
Read the part where Sanusi says there is equality (musaawa) if relation is not stipulated, but if it is stipulated, then the relative composite is more specific. Understand why.
Every relative composite is a real composite, but not vice versa unless we consider relation.
2
Understand the claim from al-Tajrid
Al-Tajrid claims real simple is absolutely more specific than relative simple. Note the two arguments provided: one based on simple being relative to its composite, and one based on composite.
3
Examine the critique
Sanusi raises a critical view: a real simple may not be a relative simple if it is not part of anything. This challenges the idea that every real simple is relative simple.
The Necessary is an example; it is a real simple but not a part of anything, so it is not relative simple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزءاً منه والمركب مركباً بالقياس إلى جزئه بمعنى كونه كلا له، وهذا المعنى غير معنى كونه ذا جزء في الجملة وهو معنى المركب الحقيقي، وإن كان في نفسه من قبيل الإضافة، وبين البسيط الحقيقي والبسيط الإضافي عموم من وجه لتصادقهما في بسيط حقيقي هو جزء من مركب (۱) . كالوحدة للعدد (۲) ، وصدق الحقيقي بدون الإضافي في بسيط حقيقي لا يتركب منه شيء، كالواجب وبالعكس في مركب وقع جزءا المركب كالجسم للحيوان وبين المركب الحقيقي والإضافي مساواة إن (۳) لم يشترط في الإضافي اعتبار الإضافة لأن كل مركب حقيقي ، فهو مركب بالقياس إلى جزئه، وبالعكس وعموم مطلقاً إن اشترط ذلك ) ، لأن مركب بالقياس إلى جزئة فهو مركب حقيقي، ولا نعكس لجواز أن لا تعتبر في الحقيقة الاضافة إلى جزئه فيكون أعم مطلقاً من الإضافي، وذكر في التجريد، أن البسيط الحقيقي أخص مطلقاً من الإضافي (0) أما الأول : فلأن كل بسيط حقيقي فهو بسيط بالقياس إلى المركب منه، ولا ينعكس لجواز أن يكون البسيط الإضافي مركباً حقيقياً، كالجسم للحيوان والجدار للبيت. وأما الثاني: فلأن كل مركب إضافي مركب حقيقي، وليس كل مركب حقيقي مركباً إضافياً لجواز أن لا يعتبر فيه الإضافة، وفيه نظر، لأن البسيط الحقيقي، قد لا يكون بسيطاً إضافياً، بأن لا يعتبر جزءاً من شيء أصلاً) . فالقول بأن المركب الحقيقي قد لا يكون إضافياً مع أن له جزءاً البتة، (۱) في (ب) جزء من (مركبات). (٦) سقط من (أ) كلمة (أصلاً) . والبسيط الحقيقي يكون إضافياً البتة، أنه لا يلزم أن يكون جزءاً من شيء أصلاً فضلاً عن اعتبار ذلك باطل قطعاً .
... of it, and the composite is composite in relation to its part in the sense of being a whole for it, and this meaning is different from the meaning of its having a part in general, which is the meaning of real composite, although in itself it is of the category of relation. And between the real simple and the relative simple there is general/specific in some respects, because they coincide in a real simple that is a part of a composite (1), like unity for number (2), and the real applies without the relative in a real simple from which nothing is composed, like the Necessary, and conversely in a composite that occurs as part of a composite, like body for animal. Commentary
This passage is a classification and analysis of the logical relationships between the terms 'simple' and 'composite' when taken in their real (haqiiqi) sense versus their relative (idaafi) sense. The author first distinguishes the two meanings of composite: a thing is called composite in relation to its part (relative composite) and also in the sense of having a part (real composite). The same distinction applies to simple. Then he explores the logical relations (general/specific, equality, etc.) between these four categories.

He begins: a thing is called composite in relation to its part, meaning it is a whole for that part; this is different from simply having a part (real composite). Next, he examines the relationship between real composite (murakkab haqiiqi) and relative composite (murakkab idaafi). If being relative does not require considering the relation to a part, then they are coextensive (musaawa). If it does require that consideration, then the relative composite is more specific than the real composite, because every relative composite is a real composite, but not every real composite is relative (since one might not consider its relation to its part).

He then cites al-Tajrid, which states that real simple is absolutely more specific than relative simple. He provides two arguments: first, every real simple is simple relative to its composite, but not conversely, because a relative simple may be a real composite (like body). Second, every relative composite is a real composite, but not every real composite is relative. However, he notes a problem: a real simple may not be a relative simple if it is not part of anything. The claim that a real composite may not be relative (despite having a part) and a real simple is always relative is criticized: it does not follow that a real simple must be a part of something; in fact, it can be entirely independent, so the claim is false. The discussion includes textual variants from different manuscripts (noted in parentheses).

Key Terms
مساواة
أعم مطلقاً
أخص مطلقاً
التجريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all real simples are also relative simples (i.e., always parts of something).
Correction: The Necessary (Allah) is a real simple but not a part of anything, so real simples are not necessarily relative simples.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between real composite and relative composite when the relation is not stipulated?
A) The real composite is absolutely more general.
B) The relative composite is absolutely more specific.
C) They are equal (musaawa).
D) They have a general/specific relationship in some respects.

Key Takeaway
The logical relationships between real and relative categories depend on whether the relative sense requires considering the relation, and even authoritative claims like that of al-Tajrid can be critically examined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen Sanusi critically examine a claim from al-Tajrid. Is there a deeply held belief in your own understanding of Islam that you have never questioned, assuming it must be correct because a respected scholar said it? Will you examine it now, even if it makes you uncomfortable?
?
If your own existence as a real composite depends on its parts, and you are relative to them, does that affect how you view your ultimate dependency on Allah? In what specific ways are you acting as if your parts are independent?

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
5 min
Parts Precede the Whole in Mind and Externally
Sanusi states the condition for composites and explains it with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental rule that parts precede the whole in both mental and external existence, and how this rule applies to examples like man and a house.

Core Idea
Parts precede the whole in both mind and reality.

This lesson introduces the foundational principle for composite entities: their parts must be prior to them both conceptually and in actual existence. Understanding this priority is essential before we can grasp the two types of independence derived from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense commentary style with footnotes embedded.
2
Identify the two domains of priority
Look for the terms ذهناً (mentally) and خارجاً (externally). Understand that the part precedes in both existence and non-existence in these domains.
The commentator clarifies that existence of the whole requires each part, and non-existence requires absence of any one part.
3
Connect the examples
The passage uses man (human) and house as examples. Trace how mental existence of man requires animal and rational, and external existence of house requires wall and roof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشترط في المركبة تقدم أجزائها ذهناً وخارجاً (قال: ولا بد من تقدم الجزء ذهناً، وخارجاً (٢)، فيلزمه الاستغناء عن الوسط في التصديق (۳) ، والواسطة في الثبوت، إلا أن الخاصة الأولى حقيقية، والأخريان إضافيتان)) (يعني بأن جزء الشيء يتقدمه وجوداً وعدماً في الذهن الخارج، أما الوجود. فبالنسبة إلى كل جزء، وأما العدم فبالنسبة إلى شيء ما من الأجزاء بمعنى أن وجود الإنسان مثلاً في العقل، يفتقر إلى وجود الحيوان والناطق، وعدمه إلى عدم أحدهما، ووجود البيت في الخارج، يفتقر إلى وجود الجدار والسقف، وعدمه إلى عدم شيء منهما ويتفرع على الأول الاستغناء الواسطة في التصديق بمعنى أن جزم العقل بثبوت الذاتي للماهية لا يتوقف على ملاحظة وسط واكتساب بالبرهان بل يجب إثباته لها . ويمتنع سلبه عنها بمجرد تصورها. وعلى الثاني : الاستغناء عن الوسط في الثبوت، بمعنى أن حصول الجزء للمركب كالجدار للبيت واللون للسواد لا يفتقر إلى سبب جديد فإن جاعل الجدار هو جاعل البيت وجاعل اللون هو جاعل السواد (۱) سقط من (أ) كلمة (أصلا) . (۲) بمعنى أن الماهية يفتقر وجودها الذهني والخارجي إلى وجود كل جزء من أجزائها حتى لو انتفى واحد من أجزائها انتفت إذ لا وجود لكل بدون الجزء فعدمها يكفي فيه عدم واحد من أجزائها ووجودها لا بد له من كل جزء. (۳) أي لا يحتاج إلى وسط وبرهان يتوصل به إلى التصديق بثبوت ذلك الجزء للماهية في الذهن. (٤) أي إنما ثبتنا للجزء بالإضافة إلى بعض ما هو خارج عن الماهية كطول بناء ؛ أي طويلاً يلاقي نفسه في السماء مقدار ألف ذراع مثلاً . فإن إدراك كون طوله هذا القدر يفتقر إلى وسط هندسي ومساحي يدرك به كقدره كبياض الجسم في الجملة فقد يفتقر إلى وساطة هو للقصار مثلا على ما تقدم وإلا فبعض ما هو خارج عن الماهية ثبتت له هاتان الخاصيتان .
It is required for the composite that its parts precede mentally and externally. (He said: And there is no escape from the part preceding mentally and externally (2), so it entails independence from the middle term in assent (3), and from the intermediate [cause] in existence, except that the first property is real, and the other two are relative. Meaning: the part of a thing precedes it in existence and non-existence in the mind and externally. As for existence, it is relative to each part; as for non-existence, it is relative to some one of the parts. That is, the existence of man, for example, in the mind depends on the existence of animal and rational, and its non-existence depends on the non-existence of one of them. And the existence of a house externally depends on the existence of wall and roof, and its non-existence depends on the non-existence of one of them. And from the first branches the independence from the middle term in assent, meaning that the mind's judgment about the belonging of the essential to the quiddity does not depend on observing a middle term and acquisition through demonstration, but rather it must be affirmed for it, and it is impossible to deny it from it merely by conceiving it. And from the second [branches] the independence from the middle [cause] in existence, meaning that the attainment of the part for the composite, like the wall for the house and the color for blackness, does not depend on a new cause; for the maker of the wall is the maker of the house, and the maker of the color is the maker of the blackness. (1) The word 'originally' is omitted from manuscript (أ). (2) Meaning that the quiddity's mental and external existence depends on the existence of each of its parts, so if one of its parts ceases, it ceases; for there is no existence of the whole without the part, so its non-existence suffices by the non-existence of one of its parts, and its existence requires every part. (3) Meaning it does not need a middle term and demonstration by which to reach assent to the belonging of that part to the quiddity in the mind. (4) Meaning we only affirm for the part in relation to something external to the quiddity, like the length of a building, i.e., a tallness reaching the sky of a thousand cubits, for example. For perceiving that this length is of this amount depends on a geometrical and mensural middle term by which to perceive its magnitude, like the whiteness of a body in general; it may depend on the mediation of something, like the short for example, as previously mentioned; otherwise, some of what is external to the quiddity has these two properties affirmed for it.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage presents a rule and its derived consequences. It begins by stating the condition for any composite (مُرَكَّبَة): its parts must be prior to it both mentally (ذهناً) and externally (خارجاً). The author then quotes the original text (قَالَ) which asserts that the part must precede in both mind and external existence, and that this priority entails two kinds of independence: independence from a middle term in assent (الاستغناء عن الوسط في التصديق) and independence from an intermediate cause in existence (الواسطة في الثبوت). However, the first property (independence in assent) is real (حقيقية), while the other two (the priority itself and independence in existence) are relative (إضافيتان).

Then a detailed explanation follows: The part precedes the whole in both existence and non-existence, in mind and externally. 'Existence' means that the whole's existence depends on the existence of each part; 'non-existence' means that the non-existence of any one part suffices for the non-existence of the whole. Examples: the existence of man in the mind requires both animal and rational; his non-existence follows from the absence of either. Similarly, a house requires both wall and roof; if either is absent, the house does not exist.

From the mental priority (the first property), we derive independence from a middle term in assent: the mind's judgment that an essential (ذاتي) belongs to a quiddity (ماهية) does not require a middle term or demonstration. It is known directly upon conceiving the quiddity. For example, 'animal' is essential to 'man'; we know immediately that man is animal without proof. Denying it is impossible once the concepts are understood.

From the external priority (the second property), we derive independence from an intermediate cause in existence: the part's existence for the composite does not require a separate cause. The maker of the part is the maker of the whole. For example, the same maker who makes the wall makes the house; the same agent who produces the color produces the blackness.

The passage concludes with textual notes and explanatory footnotes: one manuscript omits 'originally' (أصلاً) in a related context; the meaning of priority is clarified as the dependency of the quiddity's existence on each part; and a note about affirming additional properties (like length) that may require a middle term, which is outside the scope of this essential relation.

Key Terms
تقدم (taqaddum)
ذهناً (dhahnan)
خارجاً (khaarijan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for a composite entity?
A) Its parts must be posterior to it.
B) Its parts must precede it both mentally and externally.
C) Its parts must be independent of it.
D) Its parts must be unrelated to its existence.

Key Takeaway
The parts of any composite must be prior to it in both mental conception and external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own 'self' as a composite of body and soul. Which part—body or soul—do you actively prioritize in your daily routine? If the soul is a part on which your whole self depends, how does the lesson's assertion that 'the whole ceases if any part is missing' challenge the way you care for your soul today?
?
Think of a goal or project you are currently working on (spiritual, academic, or personal). Break it down into its essential 'parts' as the lesson suggests. Which part are you neglecting, knowing that the whole fails if that part is absent? What specific action will you take tomorrow to ensure that part is present?
?
The lesson teaches that concepts like 'animal' and 'rational' must precede the concept 'man' in the mind. In your understanding of Islam, do you mentally establish the foundational components (e.g., tawheed, following the Sunnah) before trying to grasp the complete picture? Or do you sometimes skip these parts? Examine your current study of aqeedah: are you building on solid 'parts', or missing a crucial one?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
4 min
Independence from Middle Term in Assent
Sanusi derives the first type of independence from mental priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mind's judgment about essential predicates belonging to a quiddity does not require a middle term or demonstration, based on the mental priority of parts.

Core Idea
Essential predications are self-evident upon conceiving the quiddity.

This lesson focuses on the first derivative of the mental priority of parts: the mind can directly affirm that an essential attribute belongs to a quiddity without needing a proof. This is a key insight in logic and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mental priority
Re-read the portion that explains how the existence of the whole in the mind requires the existence of each part. This sets the context for the derived independence.
The example of man requiring animal and rational is relevant.
2
Grasp the independence in assent
Focus on the phrase: 'the mind's judgment about the belonging of the essential to the quiddity does not depend on observing a middle term'. Understand that once you conceive 'man', you immediately know 'animal' belongs to it.
This is because 'animal' is part of the concept 'man'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why is this independence important? It means that foundational truths in logic (like genus-species relations) are known directly, not through syllogisms.

Misconception: One might think that all knowledge requires proof, even essential predications.

Why is the mind's judgment that the essential belongs to the quiddity independent of a middle term?
A) Because the essential is known through demonstration.
B) Because the part's priority in the mind makes such judgments self-evident upon conceiving the quiddity.
C) Because the maker of the part is the maker of the whole.
D) Because the part precedes the whole in external existence.

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes of a quiddity are known immediately upon conception, without need for proof.

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
Independence from Intermediate Cause in Existence
Sanusi derives the second type of independence and classifies the properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the part does not require a separate cause to exist within the composite, based on the external priority of parts, and the distinction between real and relative properties.

Core Idea
The part's existence in the composite requires no extra cause beyond the cause of the whole.

This lesson explores the second derivative of the priority of parts: in the external world, the part's existence for the composite does not need a new cause. It also clarifies which properties of priority are real versus relative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on external priority
Review the part about the house requiring wall and roof, and the general principle that the whole's external existence depends on all parts.
2
Understand independence in existence
Key phrase: 'the attainment of the part for the composite does not depend on a new cause'. The maker of the wall is the maker of the house – same cause.
Contrast with accidental properties that might require a separate cause.
3
Distinguish real vs relative properties
The passage states that the first property (independence in assent) is real (حقيقية), while the other two (priority itself and independence in existence) are relative (إضافيتان). Think about why: the first is intrinsic to the quiddity, the others depend on relation to the composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
