Conventional vs. Real Composites: The Necessity of Parts
Author refutes objection that composites need no parts by distinguishing conventional and real composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between conventional and real composites and why the need for parts is necessary only in the latter.

Core Idea
Real composites have real forms; conventional ones are only mental.

The author addresses an objection that might lead to confusion about the necessity of parts in composites. By distinguishing conventional composites (like an army) from real composites (like minerals), he clarifies that the need for parts is necessary only in real composites. This distinction is crucial for understanding how forms relate to matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the objection, answer, and the final hesitation about theriac.
2
Identify the objection
The objector gives two interpretations of 'no need.' Understand both: absolute no need, and no need among material parts.
3
Analyze the answer
The author rejects the first interpretation and accepts the second. He then distinguishes conventional composites (mental constructs) from real composites (with real forms).
Note the example of an army vs. mineral compounds.
4
Consider the hesitation
The author expresses doubt about whether theriac and oxymel produce a substantial form or remain accidental mixtures.
This shows the complexity of certain composites.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Summarize why the distinction matters: conventional composites have no external reality beyond their parts, while real composites have a unifying form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إن أريد عدم الاحتياج أصلاً فباطل ، لأن احتياج الهيئة الاجتماعية إلى الأجزاء المادية لازم قطعاً، وإن أريد الاحتياج فيما بين الأجزاء المادية، فذلك ليس بلازم في المركب الحقيقي أيضاً، كالبسائط العنصرية للمركبات المعدنية مثلاً
قلنا : المراد الأول، والصورة الاجتماعية في المركبات الاعتبارية محض اعتبار العقل، لا تحقق لها في الخارج، إذ ليس من العسكر في الخارج إلا تلك الأفراد، بخلاف المركبات الحقيقية، فإن هناك صوراً تفيض على المواد في نفس الأمر وستعرفها. وأما في مثل الترياق والسكنجين فهل يحدث صورة جوهرية هي مبدأ الآثار أو هو مجرد المزاج المخصوص الذي هو من قبيل الأعراض، وأن يكون التركيب الحقيقي، هل يكون من الجوهر والعرض ففيه تردد .
If it is said: if by 'no need' is meant absolutely no need, that is false, because the social entity [الهيئة الاجتماعية] necessarily needs material parts. And if by 'need' is meant need among the material parts themselves, that is not necessary even in a real composite, like the elemental simples for mineral compounds. We say: the first is intended. And the social form in conventional composites is a pure mental consideration; it has no realization in the external world, since there is nothing of an army in the external world except those individuals, unlike real composites, for there are forms that emanate upon matters in actuality, and you will know them. As for things like theriac and oxymel, does a substantial form arise that is the source of effects, or is it merely a particular temperament which is of the category of accidents, and that the real composition is from substance and accident? There is hesitation concerning that.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector questions the necessity of parts in a composite. The author clarifies that if 'no need' means absolutely no need, it is false because social composites need material parts. If 'need' means need among material parts, it is not necessary even in real composites like mineral compounds. The answer: the first meaning is intended. Social forms in conventional composites are pure mental considerations, not externally real—unlike real composites which have actual forms emanating on matter. The author then expresses hesitation about cases like theriac: whether composition produces a substantial form or remains an accidental mixture.

Key Terms

المركب الاعتباري
المركب الحقيقي
صورة جوهرية
المزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All composites require parts in the same way, and every composite has a real form.
Correction: Conventional composites (like an army) have no real form outside the mind; only real composites (e.g., minerals) have forms that make them one entity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The necessity of parts in a composite depends on whether it is a real composite (with a real unifying form) or merely conventional (a mental construct).

Overlapping Parts: Equality, Generality, and Partial Overlap
Author enumerates three types of overlapping parts with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of logical overlap (coincidence) among parts of a composite according to the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
Parts overlap when they logically coincide—either fully, one-way, or partially.

The author begins by dividing the parts of a composite into two main categories: overlapping and distinct. In this lesson we explore overlapping parts, which share some logical application. The classification reveals how parts can relate in terms of predication, setting the stage for understanding more complex composites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage on overlapping parts along with its English translation. Notice the three cases presented.
The author uses examples to clarify each type.
2
Identify the three subtypes
Find the three forms of overlap: equality, absolute generality-particularity, and generality from one aspect.
Each corresponds to a specific logical relation.
3
Examine the examples
For each subtype, note the example given (e.g., nourisher and grower for equality). Consider why each example fits.
Examples are taken from familiar composites.
4
Address a common misconception
The term 'overlapping' here is logical, not spatial. It refers to how predicates apply, not physical interpenetration.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم أجزاء المركب إلى متداخلة ومتبانية
قال : والأجزاء قد تتداخل بأن يكون بينها تصادق بالمساواة، أو العموم مطلقاً، أو من وجه، وقد تتباين متماثلة أو متخالفة، وجودية أو عدمية، أو مختلطة أو حقيقية، أو إضافية أو ممتزجة. أجزاء المركب تنقسم إلى متداخلة ومتباينة أما المتداخلة، فهي التي يكون بينها تصادق في الجملة إما على الوجه الكلي من الجانبين، بأن يصدق كل من الجزأين على كل ما يصدق عليه الآخر، فيكونان متساويين كالمركب من المغتذي والنامي، أو من جانب واحد بأن يصدق أحدهما على كل ما يصدق عليه الآخر من غير عكس، فيكون بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق، كالمركب من الحيوان والناطق. وإما لا على الوجه الكلي بأن يصدق كل منهما على بعض ما يصدق عليه الآخر، فيكون بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه كالمركب من الحيوان والأبيض، وأما المتباينة، فإما متماثلة كما في العشرة من الأحاد، وإما متخالفة محسوسة، كما في البلقية من السواد والبياض، أو معقولة كما في الجسم من الهيولي والصورة، أو مختلفة كما في الإنسان من البدن المحسوس، والنفس المعقولة. وقد تقسم المتخالفة إلى ما تكون للشيء مع ما عرض له من الإضافة إلى الفاعل، كالعطاء لفائدة من المعطي، أو إلى القابل كالفطوسة لتقعير في الأنف أو إلى الصورة كالأفطس الأنف فيه تقعير، أو إلى الغاية كالخاتم لحلقة يتزين بها الأصبع، وإلى ما يكون للشيء مع إضافة إلى المعلول كالخالق والرازق، وإلى ما لا يكون فيما بين العلة والمعلول وهو ظاهر وباعتبار آخر الأجزاء إما وجودية كالنفس والبدن للإنسان أو عدمية كسلب ضرورة الوجود والعدم للإمكان أو مختلطة من الوجودي والعدمي كالسابقية، وعدم المسبوقية للأولية، وأيضاً إما حقيقية كما في الإنسان من النفس والبدن أو إضافية كما في الأقرب من القرب. وزيادته، أو ممتزجة بعضها حقيقي، وبعضها إضافي كما في السرير من الأجزاء الخشبية والترتيب النسبي
Division of the parts of a composite into overlapping and distinct. He said: Parts may overlap, by way of coincidence between them by equality, or absolute generality, or generality from one aspect; and they may be distinct, being similar or different, existential or privative, or mixed, or real, or relative, or mixed. The parts of a composite are divided into overlapping and distinct. As for overlapping, those are between which there is coincidence in general: either in a universal manner from both sides, such that each of the two parts applies to everything the other applies to, so they are equal, like the composite of the nourisher and the grower; or from one side, such that one applies to everything the other applies to without reciprocity, so there is absolute generality and particularity between them, like the composite of animal and rational; or not in a universal manner, such that each applies to some of what the other applies to, so there is generality and particularity from one aspect, like the composite of animal and white. As for the distinct: either similar, as in ten from ones; or different: sensible, as in a piebald animal from black and white; or intelligible, as in a body from matter and form; or different as in a human from the sensible body and the intelligible soul. And the different may be divided into what is for a thing along with what occurs to it from an addition to the agent, like giving for the benefit of the giver, or to the recipient, like snubness due to concavity in the nose, or to the form, like a snub nose has concavity, or to the end, like a ring for a finger-ornament; and into what is for a thing with addition to the effect, like the creator and the provider; and into what is not between cause and effect, which is obvious. And by another consideration, parts are either existential, like soul and body for a human, or privative, like the negation of necessity of existence and non-existence for contingency, or mixed from existential and privative, like priority and non-priority for primacy. Also, either real, as in a human from soul and body, or relative, as in the nearer from nearness and its increase, or mixed, some real and some relative, as in a bed from wooden parts and relative arrangement.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the parts of a composite. First, parts are divided into overlapping and distinct. Overlapping parts have coincidence: (1) equality—each applies to all of the other (nourisher and grower); (2) absolute generality-particularity—one applies to all of the other but not vice versa (animal and rational); (3) partial overlap (animal and white). Distinct parts are similar (units in ten) or different. Different parts further divide: sensible (black/white), intelligible (matter/form), and based on relations to agent, recipient, form, end, effect, etc. Another division considers existential vs privative, real vs relative, and mixed. The classification is exhaustive and demonstrates the complexity of composite parts in ontology.

Key Terms
متداخلة
تصادق
مساواة
عموم وخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Overlapping parts means they physically interpenetrate or share space.
Correction: Overlapping here is logical: it refers to the way predicates (concepts) apply to objects, not spatial overlap.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of overlapping parts that are equal (متداخلة بالمساواة)?
A) Animal and white.
B) Nourisher and grower.
C) Animal and rational.
D) Body and soul.

Key Takeaway
Overlapping parts are defined by how their predicates coincide: fully, one-way, or partially.

Distinct Parts: Similar, Different, and Further Divisions
Author classifies distinct parts with multiple levels of subdivision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of distinct parts of a composite, including similar vs different, and the further divisions of different parts into relational, existential, real, etc.

Core Idea
Distinct parts can be similar or different, with differences further categorized by relation, nature, and composition.

Having completed the discussion of overlapping parts, the author now turns to distinct parts—those that do not share logical coincidence. He first divides them into similar and different, then provides a rich taxonomy of differences based on whether they are sensible, intelligible, relational, existential, real, or mixed.

D) Soul and body in a human.

Key Takeaway
Distinct parts encompass a wide range of relationships, from identical essences to various forms of difference based on relation, existence, and reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a composite of body and soul, as the passage says. Which of these two parts do you feed more in your daily life? If the soul is neglected, what specific change will you make today to prioritize your spiritual growth?
?
The passage mentions privative parts—absences that define a thing. What absence in your character (e.g., patience, gratitude) is currently defining your relationship with Allah? How will you turn that privation into an existential virtue this week?
?
You are contingent; your existence is borrowed and always dependent on Allah. Does that reality humble you, or does it slip your mind? Write down one action you will take today to remind yourself of your absolute need for Him.

Logic and Technical Terms

Types of Agreement Among Parts
Classification of parts based on agreement types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of logical agreement between parts in a composite: coextensive, absolute generality/specificity, and generality/specificity from one aspect.

Core Idea
Parts can agree in whole or in part – their logical relationship determines the composite.

This passage introduces the foundational classification of parts based on how their meanings overlap. Understanding these three types of agreement is essential for analyzing definitions and composites in logic and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on agreement
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the translation. Notice the three examples given.
The author uses specific terms for each type.
2
Identify each type
For each type, note the logical condition and the example. Coextensive: each applies to all of the other; absolute generality/specificity: one applies to all of the other but not vice versa; partial overlap: each applies to some of the other.
3
Reflect on importance
Ask yourself why this classification matters for theological definitions. These relations are the building blocks of conceptual analysis.
They are used in defining attributes of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأحاد، بينها تصادق في الجملة إما على الوجه الكلي من الجانبين، بأن يصدق كل من الجزأين على كل ما يصدق عليه الآخر، فيكونان متساويين كالمركب من المغتذي والنامي، أو من جانب واحد بأن يصدق أحدهما على كل ما يصدق عليه الآخر من غير عكس، فيكون بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق، كالمركب من الحيوان والناطق. وإما لا على الوجه الكلي بأن يصدق كل منهما على بعض ما يصدق عليه الآخر، فيكون بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه كالمركب من الحيوان والأبيض، وأما المتباينة، فإما متماثلة كما في العشرة من وإما متخالفة محسوسة، كما في البلقية من السواد والبياض، أو معقولة كما في الجسم من الهيولي والصورة، أو مختلفة كما في الإنسان من البدن المحسوس، والنفس المعقولة. وقد تقسم المتخالفة إلى ما تكون للشيء مع ما عرض له من الإضافة إلى الفاعل، كالعطاء لفائدة من المعطي، أو إلى القابل كالفطوسة لتقعير في الأنف أو إلى الصورة كالأفطس الأنف فيه تقعير، أو إلى الغاية كالخاتم لحلقة يتزين بها الأصبع، وإلى ما يكون للشيء مع إضافة إلى المعلول كالخالق والرازق، وإلى ما لا يكون فيما بين العلة والمعلول وهو ظاهر وباعتبار آخر الأجزاء إما وجودية كالنفس والبدن للإنسان أو عدمية كسلب ضرورة الوجود والعدم للإمكان أو مختلطة من الوجودي والعدمي كالسابقية، وعدم المسبوقية للأولية، وأيضاً إما حقيقية كما في الإنسان من النفس والبدن أو إضافية كما في الأقرب من القرب. وزيادته، أو ممتزجة بعضها حقيقي، وبعضها إضافي كما في السرير من الأجزاء الخشبية والترتيب النسبي
The parts, between them are in agreement in the whole either in a universal manner from both sides, such that each of the two parts applies to everything that the other applies to, so they are coextensive (mutasāwiyān), like the composite of the nourished and the growing; or from one side, such that one of them applies to everything that the other applies to without the converse, so between them is absolute generality and specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq), like the composite of animal and rational; or not in a universal manner, such that each of them applies to some of what the other applies to, so between them is generality and specificity from one aspect (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh), like the composite of animal and white. As for the distinct (mutabāyina), they are either similar (mutamāthila), as in the ten of [?], or dissimilar (mutakhālifa) sensible, as in the piebald from black and white, or intelligible, as in the body from matter and form, or different (mukhtalifa), as in man from the sensible body and the intelligible soul. And the dissimilar may be divided into what belongs to a thing along with what occurs to it of relation to the agent, like giving for the benefit of the giver; or to the recipient, like flat-nosedness (faṭūsa) due to concavity in the nose; or to the form, like the flat-nosed one whose nose has concavity; or to the end, like the ring for a finger ring to be adorned with; and into what belongs to a thing with relation to the effect, like the Creator and the Provider; and into what does not belong between cause and effect, which is apparent. And according to another consideration, the parts are either existential (wujūdiyya), like soul and body for man; or privative (ʿadamiyya), like the negation of the necessity of existence and non-existence for possibility; or mixed from existential and privative, like priority (sābiqiyya) and non-being-preceded (ʿadam al-masbūqiyya) for firstness. Also, they are either real (ḥaqīqiyya), as in man from soul and body; or relational (iḍāfiyya), as in the nearer from nearness and its increase; or mixed, some real and some relational, as in the bed from the wooden parts and the relative arrangement.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a classification of parts (ajzā') and their logical relationships. The author begins by discussing parts that are in agreement (mutasādiqa). He divides them into three types: First, coextensive parts (mutasāwiyān), where each part applies to everything the other applies to, like 'nourished' and 'growing' in a composite. Second, parts with absolute generality and specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq), where one applies to everything the other applies to but not vice versa, like 'animal' and 'rational'. Third, parts with generality and specificity from one aspect (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh), where each applies to some of what the other applies to, like 'animal' and 'white'. Then he discusses distinct parts (mutabāyina). These are divided into similar (mutamāthila), e.g., ten of something (the text is unclear); dissimilar sensible (mutakhālifa ḥissiyya), like black and white in a piebald; dissimilar intelligible (maʿqūla), like matter and form in a body; or different (mukhtalifa), like the sensible body and intelligible soul in man. The dissimilar parts are further subdivided based on relations: relation to the agent (e.g., giving to the giver), to the recipient (e.g., flat-nosedness from concavity), to the form (e.g., the flat-nosed individual), to the end (e.g., a ring for the finger), to the effect (e.g., Creator and Provider), and those without cause-effect relation. Then the author considers parts from another perspective: existential (wujūdiyya), like soul and body; privative (ʿadamiyya), like the negation of necessity for possibility; mixed (mukhtaliṭa), like priority and non-being-preceded for firstness. Finally, parts are divided into real (ḥaqīqiyya), relational (iḍāfiyya), and mixed, with examples: real like soul and body in man, relational like the nearer from nearness and its increase, mixed like a bed from wooden parts and relative arrangement.

Key Terms
أجزاء (ajzā')
متساويان (mutasāwiyān)
عموم وخصوص مطلق (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq)
عموم وخصوص من وجه (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which example illustrates coextensive parts?
A) Nourished and growing
B) Animal and rational
C) Animal and white
D) Soul and body

Key Takeaway
The three types of agreement between parts – coextensive, absolute generality/specificity, and partial overlap – are fundamental for analyzing definitions and composites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define your own beliefs or identity, do you carefully ensure that the parts are coextensive, or do you allow partial overlaps that lead to confusion? How can you apply this lesson to clarify your understanding of Islamic concepts?
?
Consider a personal relation you have: is it based on complete mutual understanding (coextensive) or partial overlap? What steps would you take to move toward fuller alignment?

Distinct Parts and Their Subdivisions
Classification of distinct parts and their relational subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of distinct parts and their subdivisions, including similar and dissimilar types, and the various relations that define dissimilar parts.

Core Idea
Distinct parts are not in agreement; they are separate but can be related through similarity, sense, or causality.

After covering parts that agree, the author turns to parts that are distinct (mutabāyina), meaning they do not apply to any common subject. These are further divided into similar and dissimilar, with the latter having multiple subdivisions based on how they relate via causality and attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on distinct parts
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the classification of distinct (mutabāyina) parts.
Note that the text has an incomplete example for 'similar' parts.
2
Identify the two main categories
Find the division into similar (mutamāthila) and dissimilar (mutakhālifa). Then note the three subtypes of dissimilar: sensible, intelligible, different.
3
Explore the relations of dissimilar parts
The author further divides dissimilar parts based on relation to agent, recipient, form, end, effect, or no relation. Understand each with its example.
These relations show how parts can be conceptually tied even without logical agreement.
4
Reflect on the importance
Consider how these distinctions help in analyzing attributes of things, especially in theology where divine attributes may be distinct but related.

Further Classifications: Existential/Privative and Real/Relational
Further classification of parts: existential/privative and real/relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional classifications of parts: existential vs. privative and real vs. relational, including mixed cases.

Core Idea
Parts can be classified by their ontological status (existent or absent) and by their nature (real or relational).

In this final classification, the author introduces two new dichotomies: whether parts are existential or privative, and whether they are real or relational. These distinctions are essential for analyzing composite entities in metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on additional classifications
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the two new divisions: existential/privative and real/relational.
Note that each division has a mixed category.
2
Understand existential vs. privative
Existential parts are positive entities; privative parts are absences. Mixed combine both. Example: priority involves existence of priority and absence of being preceded.
3
Understand real vs. relational
Real parts have intrinsic reality; relational parts exist only relative to something else. Mixed: some real, some relational, like a bed (wood and arrangement).
4
Reflect on application
Consider how these classifications help in analyzing the composition of things, especially in distinguishing essential from accidental attributes.

Source Text

D) Animal

Key Takeaway
Parts can be classified not only by logical agreement but also by whether they are existential or privative, and whether they are real or relational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own character, do you ignore the privative aspects—the lacks and absences—and focus only on your positive traits? How might acknowledging what you lack bring you closer to the truth about yourself? What one absence will you admit to Allah today?
?
Your relationships with others contain both real components (like your body) and relational components (like the bond itself). Which do you neglect? Are you maintaining the real acts while letting the relational connection weaken? Commit to one action today that strengthens the relational dimension.
?
Everything in creation is a mix of existence and non-existence, reality and relation. This means you are not self-sufficient. In what area of your life have you been pretending to be whole when you are actually a composite? What need must you turn to Allah for right now?

Refuting the Fourth Argument: The Agent's Effect is on the Quiddity
Al-Taftazani refutes the fourth argument about the agent's effect on quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the claim that the agent's effect is on the quiddity by distinguishing between the universal quiddity and its particular instances.

Core Idea
The 'made' is the individual, not the universal quiddity.

Al-Taftazani continues his responses to objections by addressing a fourth argument that insists the agent's effect must be on the quiddity itself. He presents the opponent's reasoning and then clarifies a crucial distinction: the dispute concerns quiddities as universals, not their concrete instantiations. This lesson unpacks that response and shows why the argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once through. Notice the structure: first a clarification about 'determination', then the opponent's argument, then the refutation.
Pay attention to the four options listed by the opponent.
2
Identify the opponent's four possibilities
The opponent argues that the 'made' must be one of four: quiddity, existence, the quiddity being qualified by existence, or joining of parts. List them and consider how each is itself a quiddity.
This leads to the conclusion that the 'made' is the quiddity itself.
3
Understand al-Taftazani's refutation
Al-Taftazani responds that the dispute is about quiddities as universals, not their particular instantiations. Therefore, the 'made' can be an individual instance, not the universal quiddity.
This distinction resolves the confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثالث: إن أريد بالتقرر التحقق والثبوت فهو الوجود، وإن أريد كون الماهية في نفسها تلك الماهية في الخارج، فلم يسبق ما يدل على أن ذلك بالفاعل، فالوجوه الثلاثة على تقدير تمامها لا تفيد إلا كون الوجود بالفاعل. الرابع: أنه لا نزاع في أن للعلة جعلاً وتأثيراً في الممكن فالمجعول إما الماهية أو الوجود، أو اتصاف الماهية بالوجود، أو انضمام لأجزاء بعضها إلى بعض في المركب خاصة، وكل من الأمور الأربعة ماهية من الماهيات، فيكون المجعول هو الماهية. والجواب: أن النزاع في الماهيات التي هي حقائق الأشياء، لا فيما صدقت هي عليه من الأفراد فيجوز أن يكون المجعول ذلك الشخص الذي هو من أفراد ماهية الإنسان مثلاً، أو الوجود الخاص الذي هو من أفراد ماهية الوجود كذا الاتصاف والانضمام.
And regarding the third: If by ‘determination’ is meant verification and existence, then it is existence; and if is meant the quiddity being in itself that quiddity in the external, then nothing preceded to indicate that that is by the agent. So the three aspects, on the assumption of their completeness, only yield that existence is from the agent. The fourth: There is no dispute that the cause has a making and an effect in the possible, so the ‘made’ is either the quiddity, or existence, or the quiddity’s being qualified by existence, or the joining of some parts to others in the composite specifically. And each of the four matters is a quiddity among quiddities, so the ‘made’ would be the quiddity. The answer: The dispute concerns the quiddities that are the realities of things, not what they apply to of individuals. So it is permissible that the ‘made’ be that particular which is an individual of the quiddity of man, for example, or the particular existence which is an individual of the quiddity of existence, similarly the attribution and joining.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage refutes a fourth argument from opponents who claim that the agent's effect is on the quiddity itself. The author first explains that if by 'determination' (taqrur) is meant verification or existence, then that is existence itself; if it means the quiddity in itself externally, no proof shows that is from the agent. Thus the three previous aspects only yield that existence is from the agent, not the quiddity. The opponent then argues that since the agent has an effect on the possible, the 'made' must be one of four things: quiddity, existence, the quiddity's being qualified by existence, or the joining of parts in composites. All four are quiddities themselves, so the made is the quiddity. The refutation clarifies that the dispute is about quiddities as universals (the realities of things), not about their particular instantiations. Therefore, the 'made' can be the particular individual (e.g., this man) or the particular existence, which are individuals of the quiddity, not the quiddity itself. This resolves the confusion by distinguishing between the universal nature and its concrete instances.

Key Terms
ماهية (quiddity)
مجعول (made)
علة (cause)
تقرر (determination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'made' always refers to the universal quiddity itself.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that the dispute is about quiddities as universals, but the 'made' can be a particular instance of that quiddity, not the universal nature.

Checkpoint
How does al-Taftazani refute the argument that the 'made' is the quiddity itself?
A) He denies that the agent has any effect on the possible.
B) He argues that the quiddity is uncreated and eternal.
C) He distinguishes between the quiddity as a universal and its particular instances.
D) He claims that the 'made' is always existence, never the quiddity.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani refutes the fourth argument by showing that the 'made' is the particular instance, not the universal quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson distinguishes between a universal concept and its particular instances. In your own life, are you more attached to abstract ideas (e.g., 'justice', 'success') or to the concrete realities they represent? How does confusing the two affect your actions?
?
Think of a time when you argued about a principle, forgetting that the real issue was about a specific situation. How can you train yourself to focus on the particular rather than the universal in disagreements?
?
The opponent assumed the 'made' must be a quiddity because all candidates were quiddities. Do you ever assume something must be a certain way because all examples you see fit a pattern? Recall a specific instance where this led you to a wrong conclusion. What will you do differently now?

The Dispute over the Made-ness of Quiddity: Arguments and Refutations
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the opponents' arguments that the quiddity is not made by the agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' arguments that the quiddity is not made by the agent and al-Taftazani's refutation through distinguishing the meanings of 'made-ness' and analyzing the logical implications of the agent's absence.

Core Idea
The quiddity's need for the agent is a concomitant of existence, not of the quiddity itself.

Al-Taftazani engages with a key theological dispute: whether the quiddity (essence) of a thing is made by the agent or not. Opponents argue that if humanity were from the agent, removing the agent would make man not-man, which is impossible. Taftazani clarifies the ambiguity in 'made-ness' and shows that the opponents' argument fails when the correct meaning is applied.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' initial argument
Identify the claim: if humanity were from the agent, then without the agent man would not be man, leading to impossibility.
Notice that the opponents present this as a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Understand the clarification of 'made-ness'
Taftazani explains that 'made-ness' can mean need for the agent (concomitant of existence) or need for another (concomitant of quiddity in composites). This yields three positions.
The three positions: absolute affirmation, absolute denial, and differentiation.
3
Examine the second proof and its refutation
The opponents argue that if man's being man were from the agent, it would cease when the agent ceases. Taftazani refutes by distinguishing the simple negative (true) from the modificatory (does not follow).
Key distinction: salb vs. 'adul.
4
Consider the final objection
An objection is raised about the locus of disagreement: since the agent's effect is not separate for quiddity and existence, some say only existence is made. The passage ends incomplete.
This sets up further discussion on the nature of causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل المخالفين في مجعولية الماهية. قال: قالوا لو كانت إنسانية الإنسان بالفاعل لما كان إنساناً عند عدمه، لنا اللازم السلب والمحال العدول. فإن قيل: معلوم أن ليس هنا تأثير في ماهية الممكن، وآخر في وجوده، وأن ليس لها تقرر في الخارج بدون الفاعل لها، فما وجه هذا الاختلاف. أجيب بأنه قد يراد بالمجعولية الاحتياج إلى الفاعل وهي من لوازم الوجود كتناهي الجسم دون الماهية كزوجية الأربعة، وقد يراد الاحتياج إلى الغير، فيكون من لوازم الماهية في المركب خاصة، فمن قال: بالمجعولية مطلقاً أراد عروضها للماهية في الجملة، ومن نفاها أراد أن الاحتياج إلى الفاعل ليس من عوارض الماهية، ومن فصل أراد أن الاحتياج إلى الغير من لوازم الماهية المركب دون البسيط، واشتركا في احتياج الوجود إلى الفاعل. احتج القائلون بعدم مجعولية الماهية بأن كون الإنسان إنساناً لو كان بالفاعل لارتفع بارتفاعه، فيلزم أن لا يكون الإنسان إنساناً على تقدير عدم الفاعل وهو محال. والجواب: أنه إن أريد أنه يلزم أن يكون الإنسان ليس بإنسان بطريق السلب ولا نسلم استحالته، فإن عند ارتفاع الفاعل يرتفع الوجود، وتبقى الماهية معدومة، فيكذب الإيجاب فيصدق السلب، وإن أريد بطريق العدول، بأن يتقرر الإنسان في نفسه بحسب الخارج، ويكون لا إنساناً فلا نسلم لزومه، فإن عند ارتفاع الفاعل لا يبقى الإنسان حتى يصلح موضوعاً للإيجاب. فإن قيل: يريد التنبيه على ما يصلح محلاً للخلاف في هذه المسألة، فإنه معلوم أن ليس للفاعل تأثير، وجعل بالنسبة إلى ماهية الممكن، وآخر بالنسبة إلى وجوده، حتى تكون الماهية مجعولة كالوجود، وأن ليس للماهية تقرر في الخارج بدون الفاعل حتى يكون المجعول: هو الوجود فقط، بل أثر
Proof of the opponents regarding the madeness of the quiddity. He said: They said: If the humanity of man were from the agent, then he would not be man when it [the agent] is absent; we reply: the necessary consequence is the negative, and impossible is the modificatory. For it is said: It is known that there is not here an effect on the quiddity of the possible and another on its existence, and that it has no determination in the external without the agent for it, so what is the basis of this difference? It was answered: That by ‘made-ness’ may be intended the need for the agent, which is a concomitant of existence like the finitude of body—not of the quiddity like the evenness of four; and may be intended the need for another, which is a concomitant of the quiddity specifically in the composite. So whoever affirms made-ness absolutely intends its accidentalness to the quiddity in general; whoever denies it intends that the need for the agent is not an accident of the quiddity; and whoever distinguishes intends that the need for another is a concomitant of the quiddity of the composite, not of the simple, while they share in the need of existence for the agent. Those who deny the made-ness of the quiddity argued that if man’s being man were from the agent, it would cease with his cessation, so it would follow that man would not be man on the supposition of the agent’s absence, which is impossible. The answer: If it is intended that it follows that man is not man by way of the simple negative, we do not grant its impossibility—for when the agent ceases, existence ceases, and the quiddity remains nonexistent, so the affirmative is falsified and the negative is true. If it is intended by way of the modificatory, that man is determined in himself externally and is not-man, we do not grant its implication, for when the agent ceases, man does not remain to be a subject for the affirmative. For it is said: He intends to point out what is suitable as a locus of disagreement in this issue: for it is known that the agent has no effect—a making with respect to the quiddity of the possible and another with respect to its existence—such that the quiddity would be made like existence, and that the quiddity has no determination in the external without the agent such that the made would be only existence. But the effect...
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage presents the opponents’ proofs that the quiddity (mahiyya) is not made (maj‘ul) by the agent, along with the author’s refutations. It is structured as two distinct arguments and their responses.

First, the opponents argue: if humanity were from the agent, then when the agent is absent, man would not be man—which leads either to a simple negative (man is not man) or a modificatory (man is non-man), both claimed impossible. The author responds by clarifying the ambiguity in ‘made-ness’: it can mean need for the agent (a concomitant of existence, not quiddity) or need for another (a concomitant of quiddity in composites). Thus, three positions are distinguished: absolute affirmation (need for agent attaches to quiddity in some sense), absolute denial (need for agent is not an accident of quiddity), and differentiation (need for another for composites, but not simples; both agree existence needs the agent).

Second, the opponents present another argument: if man’s being man were from the agent, it would cease when the agent ceases, leading to the impossibility of man not being man. The author answers: if by ‘not-man’ the simple negative is meant, this is not impossible, because when the agent ceases, existence ceases and the quiddity remains nonexistent—the negative is true. If the modificatory is meant (man existing as non-man), that does not follow because without the agent, man does not remain to be a subject for such a judgment.

The passage ends with an objection about the locus of disagreement: since the agent’s effect is not separate for quiddity and existence, and quiddity has no external determination without the agent, some say the made is only existence… The text breaks off.

Key Terms
ماهية (quiddity)
مجعولية (made-ness)
احتياج (need)
فاعل (agent)
لوازم (concomitants)
سلب (negative)
عدول (modificatory)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the opponents' argument is logically sound and proves the quiddity is not made.
Correction: The opponents' argument conflates the quiddity's need for the agent (a concomitant of existence) with need for another, and fails to account for the distinction between simple negative and modificatory.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the second proof, why does the author say the simple negative (salb) is not impossible?
A) Because the quiddity is eternal and cannot be destroyed.
B) Because when the agent ceases, existence ceases, so the quiddity is nonexistent and the negative is true.
C) Because the agent's effect is only on the quiddity, not on existence.
D) Because the quiddity can exist without the agent.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the made-ness of the quiddity is resolved by distinguishing the need for the agent (a concomitant of existence) from the need for another (a concomitant of the quiddity in composites).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you truly believe that your quiddity (what you are) does not, by itself, need God, but only your existence does? How does this distinction change the way you see your complete dependence on Him?
?
The opponents thought their logical argument was airtight, but Taftazani showed it relied on a confusion. Are there beliefs about your relationship with God that you hold as self-evident but might be masking a similar confusion? Will you re-examine them?
?
If the need for the agent is only a concomitant of existence, then your very existence is a constant reminder of your need for God. How often do you live as if your existence is self-sufficient? What would it look like to live every moment conscious that you are 'made' by Him?

The Createdness of Essence: Dispute or Terminology?
The author analyzes competing views on whether essence is made and resolves them terminologically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different meanings of 'createdness of essence' and how the apparent dispute between scholars is resolved by distinguishing between need for an agent and need for a part.

Core Idea
The debate over createdness of essence dissolves once we distinguish need for an agent from need for a part.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a nuanced analysis of a key theological and philosophical dispute: whether essence (mahiyyah) is 'made' (maju'liyyah). The author, al-Sanusi, navigates between the views of al-Razi and al-Iji, showing that once we distinguish between two types of need—need for an agent and need for a part—the disagreement becomes merely a matter of terminology. Let's follow this careful reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how the passage moves from stating al-Razi's view to presenting al-Iji's analysis.
The passage is dense; read slowly to absorb the distinctions.
2
Understand the two types of 'need'
Identify the two meanings of 'createdness' introduced: need for an agent (احتياج إلى الفاعل) and need for another (احتياج إلى الغير), which includes need for a part.
3
Distinguish concomitants: essence vs. identity
Learn the difference between necessary concomitants of essence (e.g., evenness of four) and of identity (e.g., finitude of body). Notice how need for agent attaches to identity, while need for part attaches to essence of composites.
This distinction is crucial for resolving the dispute.
4
See how each position is reinterpreted
Examine how the three positions (absolute affirmation, absolute denial, differentiation) are understood under this analysis: each refers to a different sense of 'made'.
5
Realize the dispute is only apparent
Conclude that once the meanings are clarified, there is no real disagreement. The passage ends by noting that no dispute has been realized in meaning.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفاعل مجعولية الماهية ، بمعنى صيرورتها موجودة، وما ذكره الإمام من أن المراد أن الماهية من حيث هي هي ليست بمجعولة، كما أنها ليست بموجودة ، ولا معدومة ولا واحدة ولا كثيرة إلى غير ذلك من العوارض، بمعنى أن شيئاً منها ليس نفسها . ولا داخلاً فيها، ليس مما يتصور فيه نزاع، أو يتعلق بتخصيصه بالذكر ،فائدة والأقرب ما ذكره صاحب المواقف، وهو أن المجعولية قد يراد بها الاحتياج إلى الغير على ما يعم الجزء، وكلاهما بالنسبة إلى الممكن من العوارض ، والعوارض منها ما يكون من لوازم الماهية ، كزوجية الأربعة. حتى لو تصورنا أربعة ليست بزوج ، لم تكن أربعة ، ومنها ما يكون من لوازم الهوية، كتناهي الجسم وحدوثه ، حتى لو تصورنا جسماً ليس بمتناه أو حادث كان جسماً ولا خفاء في أن احتياج الممكن إلى الفاعل في المركب والبسيط جميعاً من لوازم الهوية دون الماهية وأن الاحتياج إلى الغير من لوازم الماهية المركب دون البسيط، إذ لا يعقل مركب لا يحتاج إلى الجزء ، فمن قال بمجعولية الماهية مطلقاً، أي بسيطة كانت أو مركبة أراد أن المجعولية تعرض للماهية في الجملة . أعني الماهية بشرط شيء ، وهي الماهية المخلوطة ، ومرجعها إلى الهوية، وإن لم تعرض للماهية من حيث هي، ويحتمل أن يراد أنه يعرض للماهية من حيث المجعولية في الجملة، أي بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الغير، وإن لم تكن بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الفاعل ومن قال بعدم مجعولية الماهية أصلاً، أراد أن الاحتياج إلى الفاعل ليس من عوارض الماهية ، بل من عوارض الهوية، ومن فرق بين المركبة والبسيطة أراد أن الاحتياج إلى الغير من لوازم ماهية المركب دون البسيط، وإن اشتركا في الاحتياج إلى الفاعل بالنظر إلى الهوية، هذا ولكن لم يتحقق نزاع في المعنى .
The agent [makes] the createdness of essence, meaning its becoming existent. And what al-Imam mentioned — that what is meant is that essence as such is not made, just as it is not existent, nor non-existent, nor one, nor many, etc. of accidents; meaning that nothing of them is its self or internal to it — is not something in which dispute can be imagined, or [it] pertains to specification by mention. A benefit: The nearest [view] is what the author of al-Mawaqif mentioned, which is that createdness may mean need for another in a general way that includes the part, and both [need for agent and need for part] are accidents relative to the possible. Among accidents are those that are necessary concomitants of essence, like the evenness of four — even if we imagine a four that is not even, it would not be four — and among them are those that are necessary concomitants of identity, like the finitude of body and its temporality — even if we imagine a body that is not finite or not temporal, it would be a body. And it is no secret that the need of the possible for the agent in both composite and simple [essences] is from the concomitants of identity, not essence, and that the need for another is from the concomitants of the essence of the composite, not the simple, since a composite that does not need a part is inconceivable. So whoever says that essence is made absolutely — whether simple or composite — means that createdness attaches to essence in general, i.e., the essence conditioned by something, which is the mixed essence, and its referent is identity, even if it does not attach to essence as such. And it is possible that it means that it attaches to essence as such in terms of createdness in general, i.e., in the sense of need for another, even if not in the sense of need for the agent. And whoever says that essence is not made at all means that need for the agent is not an accident of essence, but rather an accident of identity. And whoever differentiates between composite and simple means that need for another is a necessary concomitant of the essence of the composite, not the simple, although they share in need for the agent with respect to identity. This [is the state of affairs], but [in reality] no dispute has been realized in meaning.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the different positions regarding the 'createdness' (maju'liyyah) of essence (mahiyyah). The author begins by noting what al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) stated: that essence as such is not made, just as it is not existent or non-existent, etc. This, he says, is not a matter of dispute. Then he presents the most plausible view from the author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji), who distinguishes two senses of 'made': need for an agent and need for another in general (including the part). Both are accidents of the possible. The author then explains the types of accidents: some are necessary concomitants of essence (like evenness of four) and some of identity (like finitude and temporality of body). He clarifies that need for an agent is a concomitant of identity for both composite and simple essences, while need for another (a part) is a concomitant of the essence of composites only. Those who affirm absolute maju'liyyah mean it for essence conditioned by existence (mixed essence), which reduces to identity. Those who deny it altogether mean need for agent is not an accident of essence. Those who differentiate mean that need for another applies to composite essence only, while both share in need for agent with respect to identity. The conclusion is that once these distinctions are made, there is no real dispute.

Key Terms
مجعولية
ماهية
هوية
لازم الماهية
لازم الهوية
احتياج إلى الفاعل
احتياج إلى الغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think 'createdness of essence' refers to a single property that one group affirms and another denies.
Correction: Actually, the term 'createdness' is ambiguous: it can mean need for an agent or need for a part. Once clarified, the different positions are seen to refer to different senses, so there is no real dispute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for differentiating between simple and composite essences regarding maju'liyyah?
A) Need for an agent is present only in composites.
B) Need for a part is a concomitant of composite essence but not simple.
C) Simple essences are not made at all.
D) Composite essences do not need an agent.

Key Takeaway
The dispute about createdness of essence is resolved by distinguishing between need for an agent (accompanying identity) and need for a part (accompanying composite essence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mistaken your innate need for God (the Agent) as something that belongs to your 'essence' rather than your 'identity'? How does distinguishing these clarify that your dependence on Him is not a part of what you are, but a constant reality of your existence?
?
If your need for the Agent is not part of your essence, does that mean you could be self-sufficient? What does the passage say about that—and what does that reveal about any pride you might harbor?
?
The passage says a composite essence necessarily needs a part. Consider your own life: what 'parts' (time, health, relationships) do you mistakenly treat as optional? How would internalizing that they are necessary concomitants of your createdness change your gratitude and reliance on God?

Ta'ayyun and Its Relation to Tamayyuz
Taftazani defines ta'ayyun and contrasts it with tamayyuz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that ta'ayyun (determination) is distinct from essence, existence, and unity, and how it relates to tamayyuz (distinction) via a general-special (umum min wajh) relationship.

Core Idea
Determination is not essence or existence; it is what marks an individual apart.

In this passage, Taftazani clarifies a subtle but crucial concept in Islamic metaphysics: ta'ayyun (determination) – the property that makes a thing a unique individual. He first distinguishes it from more familiar attributes like essence and existence, then explains how it overlaps with yet differs from tamayyuz (distinction). This lesson will guide you through these distinctions, which are foundational for understanding how universals relate to particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the technical terms: ta'ayyun, tamayyuz, mahiyyah, wujud, etc.
The footnotes provide clarifications – read them too.
2
Understand what ta'ayyun is not
Taftazani states that ta'ayyun is different from essence, existence, and unity. Why? Because those apply to universals, but ta'ayyun only applies to particulars. Example: 'the universal is an essence' is true, but 'the universal is determined' is false.
3
Grasp the role of participation in ta'ayyun vs tamayyuz
Ta'ayyun does not require considering participation with others, while tamayyuz does. This is the key difference that leads to their partial overlap.
4
Analyze the umum min wajh relationship
Ta'ayyun and tamayyuz coincide when two individuals are considered as sharing a common essence (both are distinct individuals). They separate when participation is ignored (ta'ayyun applies alone) or when distinction is universal (tamayyuz applies alone).
This is a non-technical explanation of a technical logical relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : التعين يغاير الماهية، والوجود (۱) والوحدة لصدقها على الكلي دونه (۲) ولا يلزم فيه اعتبار المشاركة (۳) بخلاف التمايز، فيتصادقان إذا اعتبر مشاركة الشخصين، ويتفارقان إذا لم تعتبر المشاركة أو كان التميز كلياً فبينهما عموم من وجه .

تعين الشيء وتشخصه الذي به يمتاز عن جميع ما عداه غير ماهيته ووجوده وحدوثه، لكون كل من هذه الأمور مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره، بخلاف التعين، ولذا يصدق قولنا : الكلي ماهية وموجود وواحد . ولا يصدق قولنا إنه متعين، وإن كان التعين أو المتعين مفهوماً كلياً صادقاً على الكثرة، وبين التعين والتمييز عموم من وجه لتصادقهما على تشخصات الأفراد إذا اعتبر مشاركتها في الماهية مثلاً ، فإن كلاً منها متشخص في نفسه ومتميز عن غيره ويصدق التعين دون التميز حيث لا تعتبر المشاركة، وبالعكس حيث تتميز الكليات كالأنواع المعتبرة اشتراكها في الجنس .

(۱) لأنه ليس نفس الماهية ، وليس نفس الوجود.
(۲) أي دون التعيين فإنه لا يصدق إلا على المعنى الجزئي والشخص المعين.
(۳) المشاركة في الماهية بمعنى أن المتعين يعرض له التعيين ويوصف به من غير أن يشترط في عروضه كونه مشاركاً لمتعين .
First Section: Determination (ta'ayyun) is different from essence (mahiyyah), existence (wujud) (1) and unity (wahdah) because they apply to the universal without it (2), and consideration of participation (musharakah) is not necessary in it (3) unlike distinction (tamayyuz). So they (ta'ayyun and tamayyuz) coincide when participation of two persons is considered, and they separate when participation is not considered or when the distinction is universal; thus between them there is general-special (general and special) relation.

The determination (ta'ayyun) of a thing and its individuation (tashakhkhus) by which it is distinguished from everything else is not its essence, existence, or occurrence (huduth), because each of these matters is shared between it and others, unlike determination. Therefore our statement is true: 'The universal is an essence, existent, and one.' And our statement that it is determined (muta'ayyin) is not true, even though determination or the determined is a universal concept applying to many. And between determination (ta'ayyun) and distinction (tamyiz) there is a general-special relation, because they coincide on the individuations (tashakhkhusat) of individuals when their participation in essence, for example, is considered — for each of them is individuated in itself and distinguished from others — and determination applies without distinction where participation is not considered, and vice versa where universals are distinguished, like species whose sharing in genus is considered.

(1) Because it is not the same as essence, and not the same as existence.
(2) That is, without determination (al-ta'yin), for it applies only to the particular meaning and the specific person.
(3) Participation in essence meaning that the determined thing is subject to determination and is described by it without it being required in its occurrence that it be sharing with another determined thing.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of ta'ayyun (determination) and clarifies its relationship with tamayyuz (distinction). The author begins by stating that ta'ayyun is different from mahiyyah (essence), wujud (existence), and wahdah (unity). The reason given is that these three apply to the universal (al-kulli) without ta'ayyun applying to it; for example, we can say 'the universal is an essence, existent, and one' but we cannot say 'it is determined.' The author then notes that in ta'ayyun, consideration of participation (musharakah) is not necessary — meaning that a thing can be determined without being compared to others — unlike tamayyuz, which requires distinction from others. Next, the author explains that ta'ayyun and tamayyuz have a 'general-special' (umum min wajh) relationship: they coincide when considering the individuations (tashakhkhusat) of individuals where participation in essence is considered (e.g., two individuals both have determination and are distinct from each other), but they diverge when participation is not considered (then ta'ayyun applies without tamayyuz) or when the distinction is universal (then tamayyuz applies without ta'ayyun, as with species distinguished by genus). The footnotes further clarify: (1) ta'ayyun is not identical to essence or existence; (2) ta'ayyun only applies to particular individuals, not universals; (3) participation in essence is not a condition for ta'ayyun to occur.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun)
تشخص (tashakhkhus)
تمييز/تمايز (tamyiz/tamayyuz)
ماهية (mahiyyah)
وجود (wujud)
وحدة (wahdah)
كلية (kulliyyah)
جزئية (juz'iyyah)
عموم من وجه (umum min wajh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'ayyun (determination) is the same as essence or existence.
Correction: Essence, existence, and unity apply to universals, while ta'ayyun applies only to particular individuals. They are conceptually distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between ta'ayyun (determination) and tamayyuz (distinction)?
A) They are identical in meaning.
B) They are completely different with no overlap.
C) They have a general-special (umum min wajh) relationship.
D) Tamayyuz is a type of ta'ayyun.

Key Takeaway
Ta'ayyun is what makes an individual unique, and it partially overlaps with tamayyuz in a umum min wajh relationship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that your ta'ayyun (determination) is not your essence or existence, but what makes you uniquely you before Allah. If you were to lose everything you consider your identity—your name, your status, your accomplishments—would your ta'ayyun remain? Does your sense of self ultimately rest on what is shared (like being human) or on your unique determination that only Allah knows? How does this reshape your understanding of true accountability?
?
If ta'ayyun does not require participation (sharing with others), then your personal responsibility before Allah is absolute—it cannot be diluted by comparisons to others. When you stand before Him tomorrow, you will not be judged as 'one of the believers' but as your determined self. Have you been living as if your deeds blend into a collective, hiding behind the crowd? What one concrete change will you make today to act as the unique individual you are?
?
Consider the concept of tamayyuz (distinction) that requires comparison. The lesson says tamayyuz is not the same as ta'ayyun. In your daily life, do you measure yourself by how you differ from others (tamayyuz) rather than your God-given uniqueness (ta'ayyun)? Pride comes from tamayyuz, humility from recognizing your ta'ayyun is a gift. Will you spend this week asking Allah to show you who you truly are in His sight, apart from all comparisons?

beginner
4 min
Ta'ayyun is Distinct from Quiddity, Existence, and Oneness
Definition of ta'ayyun and its distinction from mahiya, wujud, and wahda.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ta'ayyun (determination) is not the same as mahiya (quiddity), wujud (existence), or wahda (oneness), and how these are universal concepts while ta'ayyun is particular.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun is what makes a thing particular; universals like existence are shared.

This passage establishes a key distinction in philosophical theology: the concept of ta'ayyun (determination/particularization) is not identical to other familiar notions like quiddity, existence, or unity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping subsequent discussions about divine attributes and individuation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the author's assertion that ta'ayyun differs from the three universals.
Notice the footnotes clarify the meaning.
2
Identify the three universals
Mahiya (quiddity), wujud (existence), and wahda (oneness) are all universal concepts that apply to the universal, e.g., 'human' has a quiddity, exists, and is one.
3
Understand why ta'ayyun is different
Ta'ayyun only applies to particulars, not universals. For example, the universal 'human' is not 'determined' as a specific individual.
4
Reflect on the footnote
The footnote distinguishes ta'ayyun from ta'yin (specific act of determination) and emphasizes that ta'ayyun is not the same as quiddity or existence.
This prevents a common confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
التعين
المبحث الأول : التعين يغاير الماهية، والوجود (١) والوحدة لصدقها على الكلي دونه (٢) ولا يلزم فيه اعتبار المشاركة (٣) بخلاف التمايز، فيتصادقان إذا اعتبر مشاركة الشخصين، ويتفارقان إذا لم تعتبر المشاركة أو كان التميز كلياً فبينهما عموم من وجه .
تعين الشيء وتشخصه الذي به يمتاز عن جميع ما عداه غير ماهيته ووجوده وحدوثه، لكون كل من هذه الأمور مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره، بخلاف التعين، ولذا يصدق قولنا : الكلي ماهية وموجود وواحد . ولا يصدق قولنا إنه متعين، وإن كان التعين أو المتعين مفهوماً كلياً صادقاً على الكثرة، وبين التعين والتمييز عموم من وجه لتصادقهما على تشخصات الأفراد إذا اعتبر مشاركتها في الماهية مثلاً ، فإن كلاً منها متشخص في نفسه ومتميز عن غيره ويصدق التعين دون التميز حيث لا تعتبر المشاركة، وبالعكس حيث تتميز الكليات كالأنواع المعتبرة اشتراكها في الجنس .
(١) لأنه ليس نفس الماهية ، وليس نفس الوجود.
(٢) أي دون التعيين فإنه لا يصدق إلا على المعنى الجزئي والشخص المعين.
(٣) المشاركة في الماهية بمعنى أن المتعين يعرض له التعيين ويوصف به من غير أن يشترط في عروضه كونه مشاركاً لمتعين .
The First Inquiry: Determination (ta'ayyun) is different from quiddity (mahiya) and existence (wujud) and oneness (wahda) because they are true of the universal without it (i.e., ta'ayyun), and participation need not be considered for it, unlike differentiation (tamayyuz). They coincide when participation of two persons is considered, and they diverge when participation is not considered or when the differentiation is universal, so between them is a general-and-specific relationship in one respect. The determination (ta'ayyun) of a thing and its individuation (tashakhkhus) by which it is distinguished from everything else is not its quiddity, existence, or occurrence, because each of these is shared between it and another, unlike determination. Hence we say: the universal is a quiddity, an existent, and one. But we do not say that it is determined, even though determination or the determined (muta'ayyin) is a universal concept true of many. Between determination and differentiation is a general-and-specific relationship in one respect: they coincide on the individuations of individuals when their sharing in quiddity is considered, for each of them is individuated in itself and differentiated from another; and determination is true without differentiation where participation is not considered, and the reverse where universals are differentiated, like species whose sharing in genus is considered. (1) Because it is not the essence of quiddity and not the essence of existence. (2) i.e., without determination (ta'yin), for it (ta'yin) is only true of a particular meaning and a specific person. (3) Participation in quiddity means that the determined thing is subject to determination and described by it without requiring that its occurrence involve its being a participant with another determined thing.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. It defines ta'ayyun (determination or particularization) and distinguishes it from mahiya (quiddity), wujud (existence), and wahda (oneness). The author explains that these three are universal concepts that apply to the universal, whereas ta'ayyun applies only to the particular. The passage then discusses the relationship between ta'ayyun and tamayyuz (differentiation). They are related but not identical: they coincide when we consider individuals sharing in a common quiddity (each is determined and differentiated), but differ when participation is not considered (ta'ayyun applies without tamayyuz) or when the differentiation is of universals (tamayyuz applies without ta'ayyun). The footnotes clarify that ta'ayyun is not the same as ta'yin (which is a specific act of determination) and that participation is not a necessary condition for ta'ayyun.

Key Terms
التعين
الماهية
الوجود
الوحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'ayyun is the same as ta'yin (act of determination).
Correction: Ta'ayyun is the property of being determined, while ta'yin is the act; the footnote distinguishes them.

Checkpoint
Why is ta'ayyun different from mahiya, wujud, and wahda?
A) Because they are universal and ta'ayyun is particular.
B) Because they are particular and ta'ayyun is universal.
C) Because ta'ayyun is not a real concept.
D) Because they are all identical.

Key Takeaway
Ta'ayyun is a particularizing concept, unlike universal quiddity, existence, or oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your particular existence (ta'ayyun) is just a generic human existence, or do you realize that Allah has given you a specific, unrepeatable set of circumstances for which you will be individually accountable? What would change if you truly believed that your particularity is not an accident but a divine appointment?
?
If ta'ayyun is what makes you uniquely you before Allah, then your deeds are not judged against a universal standard alone but in the context of your specific abilities and trials. Are you making excuses using universal human weakness, or are you responding to your particular ta'ayyun with gratitude and action?

Logic and Technical Terms

The Relationship between Ta'ayyun and Tamayyuz
Comparison of ta'ayyun and tamayyuz and their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how ta'ayyun (determination) and tamayyuz (differentiation) are related: they coincide under some conditions and diverge under others, forming a general-and-specific relationship.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun and tamayyuz are not identical; they overlap partially.

After establishing that ta'ayyun is distinct from universal concepts, the author now compares it with tamayyuz (differentiation). These two terms are often confused, but they have different conditions of applicability. This passage clarifies their relationship by showing when they agree and when they differ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the comparison between ta'ayyun and tamayyuz.
The passage also reiterates the distinction from universals.
2
Identify when they coincide
They coincide when considering individuals sharing in quiddity—each individual is both determined and differentiated.
3
Identify when they diverge
They diverge in two cases: when participation is not considered (ta'ayyun applies without tamayyuz), and when differentiation is of universals (tamayyuz applies without ta'ayyun).
For example, a species is differentiated but not a particular individual.
4
Understand the overall relationship
The overall relationship is 'umum min wajh: they share some instances but differ in others.
This is a common logical relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول
التعين
المبحث الأول : التعين يغاير الماهية، والوجود (١) والوحدة لصدقها على الكلي دونه (٢) ولا يلزم فيه اعتبار المشاركة (٣) بخلاف التمايز، فيتصادقان إذا اعتبر مشاركة الشخصين، ويتفارقان إذا لم تعتبر المشاركة أو كان التميز كلياً فبينهما عموم من وجه .
تعين الشيء وتشخصه الذي به يمتاز عن جميع ما عداه غير ماهيته ووجوده وحدوثه، لكون كل من هذه الأمور مشتركاً بينه وبين غيره، بخلاف التعين، ولذا يصدق قولنا : الكلي ماهية وموجود وواحد . ولا يصدق قولنا إنه متعين، وإن كان التعين أو المتعين مفهوماً كلياً صادقاً على الكثرة، وبين التعين والتمييز عموم من وجه لتصادقهما على تشخصات الأفراد إذا اعتبر مشاركتها في الماهية مثلاً ، فإن كلاً منها متشخص في نفسه ومتميز عن غيره ويصدق التعين دون التميز حيث لا تعتبر المشاركة، وبالعكس حيث تتميز الكليات كالأنواع المعتبرة اشتراكها في الجنس .
(١) لأنه ليس نفس الماهية ، وليس نفس الوجود.
(٢) أي دون التعيين فإنه لا يصدق إلا على المعنى الجزئي والشخص المعين.
(٣) المشاركة في الماهية بمعنى أن المتعين يعرض له التعيين ويوصف به من غير أن يشترط في عروضه كونه مشاركاً لمتعين .
The First Inquiry: Determination (ta'ayyun) is different from quiddity (mahiya) and existence (wujud) and oneness (wahda) because they are true of the universal without it (i.e., ta'ayyun), and participation need not be considered for it, unlike differentiation (tamayyuz). They coincide when participation of two persons is considered, and they diverge when participation is not considered or when the differentiation is universal, so between them is a general-and-specific relationship in one respect. The determination (ta'ayyun) of a thing and its individuation (tashakhkhus) by which it is distinguished from everything else is not its quiddity, existence, or occurrence, because each of these is shared between it and another, unlike determination. Hence we say: the universal is a quiddity, an existent, and one. But we do not say that it is determined, even though determination or the determined (muta'ayyin) is a universal concept true of many. Between determination and differentiation is a general-and-specific relationship in one respect: they coincide on the individuations of individuals when their sharing in quiddity is considered, for each of them is individuated in itself and differentiated from another; and determination is true without differentiation where participation is not considered, and the reverse where universals are differentiated, like species whose sharing in genus is considered. (1) Because it is not the essence of quiddity and not the essence of existence. (2) i.e., without determination (ta'yin), for it (ta'yin) is only true of a particular meaning and a specific person. (3) Participation in quiddity means that the determined thing is subject to determination and described by it without requiring that its occurrence involve its being a participant with another determined thing.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. It defines ta'ayyun (determination or particularization) and distinguishes it from mahiya (quiddity), wujud (existence), and wahda (oneness). The author explains that these three are universal concepts that apply to the universal, whereas ta'ayyun applies only to the particular. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'ayyun and tamayyuz are identical terms.
Correction: They are not identical; they have a general-and-specific relationship: they coincide in some cases and diverge in others.

Under what condition do ta'ayyun and tamayyuz coincide?
A) When considering individuals sharing in quiddity.
B) When considering universals like species.
C) When participation is not considered.
D) They never coincide.

Ta'ayyun and tamayyuz have a general-and-specific relationship in one respect: they coincide on individuals sharing quiddity but diverge otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you confused something's unique identity (ta'ayyun) with its mere difference from others (tamayyuz). How did that confusion affect your understanding of that thing? What steps will you take to ensure you distinguish between them in your thinking about people and ideas?
?
The passage shows that ta'ayyun can exist without tamayyuz when participation is not considered. Examine your own heart: are there beliefs or practices you hold that you consider uniquely 'yours' without acknowledging their connection to the wider community? How might that spiritual isolation be harming your relationship with Allah?

Logic and Technical Terms

The Two Proofs that Ta'ayyun is a Mental Consideration
Two proofs that ta'ayyun is a mental consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs that determination (ta'ayyun) is a mental consideration (i'tibari) rather than an extra-mental entity, including how the objections are answered.

Core Idea
Determination cannot be real without leading to infinite regress or circularity.

In this passage, the author establishes a key principle of kalam ontology: that the determination of a thing (its being this particular instance) is not a real entity in the external world but a mental consideration. He provides two proofs, each with objections and replies, to demonstrate that ta'ayyun must be i'tibari.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the structure: claim, first proof with two objections and replies, then second proof. Pay attention to the footnotes for additional definitions.
The footnotes clarify key terms like ta'ayyun and the analogy of 'ayn.
2
Understand the first proof (infinite regress)
The first proof argues that if ta'ayyun were a real entity, it would itself require a determination to be distinct, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Ask yourself why the argument assumes this is impossible.
In kalam, infinite regress is considered rationally impossible.
3
Examine the objections and replies
Two objections are raised: (1) sharing in ta'ayyun is merely verbal or accidental, and (2) ta'ayyun is identical to its subject. Read the author's replies carefully: he argues that every determination has a quiddity in the intellect, and that in real things subject and attribute must be distinct.
The second objection's reply actually proves ta'ayyun is i'tibari.
4
Understand the second proof (circularity)
The second proof argues that if ta'ayyun were real, its joining to a particular individual would itself require distinction, leading to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress. See how this reinforces the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
التعين اعتباري
قال : المبحث الثاني : التعين (١) اعتباري لوجهين : الأول : أنه لو وجد لكان له تعين وتسلسل .
فإن قيل : المحوج إلى التمايز بالتعين هو الاشتراك في الماهية، واشتراك التعين لفظي (٢) أو عرضي
قلنا : كل تعين فله عند العقل ماهية سواء تعددت أفرادها (٣) أو لا . فإذا وجدت في الخارج لزم التعين بالضرورة.
فإن قيل : تعينه عينه
قلنا : فيكون اعتبارياً إذ تغاير المعروض والعارض في الأمور العينية ضروري.
(٤)
الثاني : أنه لو وجد ) لتوقف انضمامه إلى حصة الشخص (ه) من النوع على تميزه فيدور أو يتسلسل .
(١) التعين : الذي به يكون الشيء غير قابل للشركة عند المتكلمين.
(٢) فصدقه عليها كصدق العين على الباصرة، والذهب فكما تمايز مصدوق الذهب والفضة بالماهية فكذا مصدوق التعين فلا يحتاج إلى التمييز بتعيين آخر لحصوله بالذات .
(٣) بأن وجدت في الخارج بالفعل.
(٤) في الخارج .
(٥) أي إلى الحصة التي هي الشخص الكائن.
The Second Inquiry: Determination (ta'ayyun) is a mental consideration (i'tibari) for two reasons: First: if it existed, it would have a determination and there would be infinite regress (tasalsul). If it is said: what necessitates differentiation by determination is sharing in quiddity, and the sharing of determination is verbal or accidental; we say: every determination has, in the intellect, a quiddity whether its individuals are multiple or not. So if it exists in the external world, determination necessarily follows. If it is said: its determination is identical to it; we say: then it is mental consideration, because the distinction between subject and attribute in real things is necessary. Second: if it existed, its joining to the share of the person from the species would depend on its being distinguished, leading to circularity or infinite regress. (1) Determination: that by which a thing is incapable of being shared, according to the mutakallimun. (2) So its truth of them is like the truth of 'ayn (in the sense of 'eye' or 'gold') on the seeing eye and gold; just as the referents of gold and silver are distinguished by quiddity, so the referent of determination does not need to be distinguished by another determination because it occurs essentially. (3) That is, if they actually exist externally. (4) In the external world. (5) i.e., to the share which is the existing person.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage is a proof that ta'ayyun (determination) is i'tibari (a mental consideration) rather than a real external entity. The author gives two proofs. The first proof: if ta'ayyun were real, it would itself require a determination to be distinct, leading to an infinite regress. An objection is raised: the need for differentiation by determination arises from sharing in quiddity, but the sharing of determination is merely verbal or accidental. The author replies: every determination has a quiddity in the intellect, and if its individuals exist externally, determination necessarily follows. Another objection: the determination of a thing is identical to the thing itself. The author replies: if so, then it is a mental consideration because in real things the subject and attribute must be distinct. The second proof: if determination existed, its joining to the particular individual would depend on its own distinction, leading to circularity or infinite regress. The footnotes define ta'ayyun in kalam terms and clarify the analogies.

Key Terms
التعين
اعتباري
تسلسل
دور
الاشتراك في الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: I'tibari means something is unreal or fictitious.
Correction: In kalam, i'tibari means it is a mental consideration (a concept that does not have independent extra-mental existence), not that it is unreal or false.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that ta'ayyun is i'tibari?
A) If ta'ayyun existed, it would have a quiddity and thus require another ta'ayyun.
B) Ta'ayyun is identical to its subject.
C) Ta'ayyun is a universal.
D) Ta'ayyun cannot be shared.

Key Takeaway
Ta'ayyun (determination) is not a real entity but a mental consideration; otherwise infinite regress or circularity would follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage argues that your individual identity (ta'ayyun) is not ultimately real but a mental construct. Does this insight shake your attachment to your ego? How would your daily actions change if you truly believed that your separate self is a mental consideration?
?
If ta'ayyun were real, infinite regress would follow. Are there beliefs you hold that, if examined, would lead to similar contradictions? Will you apply the same logical scrutiny to your own convictions as this passage applies to ta'ayyun?
?
The first proof's objection says sharing in determination is merely verbal. Are there concepts you treat as real that are actually only words with no extra-mental reality? What would it mean to act on that distinction?
