فقيل : العدمي المعدوم، وقيل ما يكون عدماً مطلقاً (۱)، أو مضافاً متركباً، مع وجودي كعدم البصر عما من شأنه، أو غير متركب كعدم قبول الشركة، وقيل، ما يدخل في مفهومه العدم ككون الشيء بحيث (۲) لا يقبل الشركة. والوجودي بخلافه، فهو الموجود، أو الوجود مطلقاً، أو مضافاً، أو ما لا يدخل في مفهومه العدم، والعبرة بالمعنى دون اللفظ، حتى إن العمى عدمي، واللاعدم وجودي، وفي المواقف أن الوجودي ما يكون ثبوته لموصوفه بوجوده له ، أي بحسب الخارج . نحو السواد لا أن يكون ذلك باعتبار وجودهما في العقل واتصاف موصوفه به فيه (۳) أي في العقل دون الخارج كالإمكان (٤)، وهو أعم من الموجود، لجواز وجودي لا يعرض له الوجود أبداً، لكنه بحيث إذا ثبت للموصوف، كان ذلك بوجوده له . وهذا معنى ما قال القاضي الأرموي، إذا قلنا لشيء إنه وجودي، لا نعني أنه دائم الوجود ، بل نعني أنه مفهوم يصح أن يعرض له الوجود الخارجي عند قيامه بموجود، وعند قيامه بمعدوم لا يكون له وجود وكأنه يريد الأعم من وجه، وإلا فمن الموجود ما لا يسمى وجودياً كالانسان، وغيره من المفهومات المستقلة، وأما الاعتباري فهو ما لا تحقق له إلا بحسب فرض العقل، وإن كان موصوفه متصفاً به في نفس الأمر كالإمكان، فإن الإنسان متصف به في نفس الأمر، بمعنى أنه بحيث إذا نسبه العقل إلى الوجود يعقل له وصفاً هو الإمكان، ويقابله الحقيقي إذا تقرر هذا فلا خفاء في أن العوارض المشخصة وجودية، والهذية اعتبارية، وتميز الفرد عما عداه وعدم قبوله الشركة وكونه ليس غيره أو لا يقبل الشركة عدمية .
(٤) الإمكان في اللغة : مصدر أمكن إمكاناً والامكان في الشيء عند المتقدمين هو اظهار ما في قوله إلى الفعل. قال ابن سينا والإمكان إما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب ضرورة العدم وهو الامتناع واما أن يعني به ما يلازم سلب الضرورة في العدم والوجود جميعاً. راجع) الاشارات: ٣٤)، فاعتبار الذات وحدها لا يخلو إما أن يكون مقتضياً لوجوب الوجود أو مقتضياً لامتناع الوجود . (راجع النجاة ص ٣٦٧). ونحن نسمي امكان الوجود قوة الوجود (راجع الشفاء ٢ : ٤٧٧) .
It was said: the privative (al-'adami) is the non-existent (al-ma'dum), and it was said: it is what is absolute non-existence, or relative and composite, along with an existential like the lack of sight in that which is capable of it, or non-composite like the non-reception of partnership. And it was said: it is what enters into its concept non-existence, like a thing being such that it does not admit partnership. And the existential (al-wujudi) is the opposite of that: it is the existent (al-mawjud), or absolute existence, or relative existence, or what does not enter into its concept non-existence, and the consideration is by meaning not by wording, so that blindness is privative, and non-privation is existential. And in al-Mawaqif: the existential is that whose affirmation for its subject is by its existence for it, i.e., according to externals, like blackness; not that this is by consideration of their existence in the intellect and the subject's being qualified by it therein, i.e., in the intellect not in externals, like possibility (al-imkan). And it is more general than the existent, because it is permissible that an existential never be attained by existence, but it is such that when it is established for the subject, that is by its existence for it. And this is the meaning of what al-Qadi al-Urmawi said: when we say of something that it is existential, we do not mean that it is eternally existent, but we mean that it is a concept which it is valid for external existence to occur to it when it subsists in an existent, and when it subsists in a non-existent it has no existence, and it is as if he intends the more general in some respect; otherwise, among existents there is what is not called existential, like man and other independent concepts. As for the relational (al-i'tibari), it is that which has no realization except according to a supposition of the intellect, even if its subject is qualified by it in reality, like possibility: for man is qualified by it in reality, meaning that he is such that when the intellect relates him to existence, it conceives for him a description which is possibility. And its opposite is the real (al-haqiqi). When this is established, there is no hiddenness: the individuating accidents are existential, and the relationals are relational, and the distinctness of the individual from what is other than it, and its non-reception of partnership, and its being not other, or its not admitting partnership, are privative.
(4) Possibility (al-imkan) in language is the masdar of 'amkana imkanan, and possibility in a thing according to the early scholars is the manifestation of what is in his statement to action. Ibn Sina said: possibility either means what entails the negation of the necessity of non-existence, which is impossibility, or what entails the negation of necessity in both non-existence and existence. Cf. al-Isharat: 34. For the consideration of the essence alone is either necessitating the necessity of existence or necessitating the impossibility of existence. (Cf. al-Najat p. 367). And we call possibility of existence potentiality of existence (Cf. al-Shifa 2:477).
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies attributes into privative ('adami) and existential (wujudi), and then distinguishes between relational (i'tibari) and real (haqiqi) within the existential category. The author begins by presenting various opinions on what 'adami means: some say it is the non-existent, others say it is absolute non-existence, or relative and composite (like lack of sight when sight is expected), or non-composite (like not admitting partnership). The defining characteristic is that non-existence enters into its concept. The existential is its opposite: it is either the existent itself, or absolute or relative existence, or what does not involve non-existence in its concept. The key point is that the classification depends on meaning, not wording—e.g., blindness is privative even though it is described as a 'lack' of sight, while non-privation is existential. The author then cites al-Mawaqif to clarify that an existential attribute is one whose affirmation for its subject is due to its existence externally (like blackness), not merely in the intellect. Possibility (imkan) is an example of something existential that is more general than the existent, because it can be predicated even if it never actually exists, but if it does exist, it does so externally. This is in line with al-Qadi al-Urmawi's view that 'existential' does not mean always existing, but means a concept that can receive external existence when attached to an existent. Then the relational (i'tibari) is defined as something that has no realization except by mental supposition, even though its subject is truly qualified by it in reality—again using possibility as an example. The real (haqiqi) is its opposite. From this, it becomes clear that individuating accidents (like specific characteristics) are existential, while relational properties are i'tibari. Furthermore, things like an individual's distinctness from others, not admitting partnership, and being not-other are all privative attributes. The footnote on imkan elaborates on its linguistic and technical definition, citing Ibn Sina and distinguishing two meanings: one that entails negation of necessity of non-existence (impossibility) and one that entails negation of necessity of both existence and non-existence.

Misconception: One might think that 'adami attributes are simply non-existent things, but they are real descriptions like blindness.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'adami attributes involve non-existence in their concept, but they are still meaningful predicates applied to subjects (e.g., blindness is a real quality of a person).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a relational (i'tibari) attribute from a real (haqiqi) attribute?
A) The relational is always privative.
B) The relational has no realization except in mental supposition, even if its subject is truly qualified.

D) The relational depends on external existence.

Key Takeaway
Privative attributes involve non-existence in their concept, while existential attributes involve existence; among the latter, relational attributes are mentally conceived but apply to real subjects, like possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your character is made of attributes. Which of your flaws are privative—like the absence of patience—and which are existential—like pride? How does this distinction change how you seek to change them, if at all?
?
The lesson says relational attributes depend on mental supposition yet are real in themselves. Consider your most deeply held belief about your own identity. Is it a real (haqiqi) attribute grounded in external reality, or a relational (i'tibari) one sustained only by your thoughts? What would happen if you stopped mentally affirming it?
?
Possibility (imkan) is a relational attribute that means 'could be otherwise'. In your relationship with Allah, have you reduced obedience to a mere possibility—something that could happen but doesn't? What would it take for your faith to become a real (haqiqi) attribute in your life?

Why Combining Universals Does Not Yield Particularity
Author explains and defends why two universals cannot combine to form a particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why combining two universal concepts cannot produce a particular concept, and how the author refutes an objection.

Core Idea
Two universals combined remain universal, never particular.

In this passage, the author deepens the earlier point about determination by clarifying a potential confusion. If universality means the mind can apply a concept to many, then combining two such concepts cannot suddenly restrict it to one. The author addresses a clever objection and shows why the composite remains universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'فإن قيل' and 'قلنا'.
2
Understand the condition for particularity
Identify what makes a concept particular: the mind cannot suppose it true of many. This is 'imtina' al-shirkah' (impossibility of sharing).
This is the key criterion.
3
Follow why combining universals fails
The author states that each element (combined, combined-to, combination) is universal, so the composite is mentally shareable. Even if only one external instance exists, the concept remains universal.
Example: the concept of the Necessary (God) is universal in thought.
4
Analyze the objection and the responses
The objection says the whole might differ from its parts. The author gives two responses: first, that describing a universal with another universal does not make it particular; second, that the claim is about necessity, not possibility.
The second response acknowledges that a composite might coincidentally be particular, but it is not entailed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد في التعين من كون المفهوم بحيث لا يمكن للعقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين، وهذا معنى امتناع الشركة ذهناً، ومعلوم أنه لا يحصل بانضمام الكلي إلى الكلي، لأن كلاً من المنضم والمنضم إليه والانضمام لكونه كلياً، يمكن للعقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين، بل على ما لا يتناهى من الأفراد، وإن كان بحسب الخارج ربما لا يوجد منه الأفراد، بل يمتنع تعدده كمفهوم الواجب. فإن قيل: حكم الكلي قد تخالف حكم كل واحد، فيجوز أن يكون كل من المنضم والمنضم إليه كلياً، والمجموع جزئياً. قلنا: لا معنى للانضمام ههنا سوى أن العقل يعتبر مفهوماً كلياً كالإنسان، ثم يعتبر له وصفاً كلياً كالفاضل ومعلوم بالضرورة أن الكلي الموصوف بالأوصاف الكلية لا ينتهي إلى حد الهذية، حتى لو كان ذلك الوصف هو مفهوم الجزئية والتشخص وامتناع قبول الشركة، كانت الكلية بحالها. وقد يجاب: بأن المراد أن انضمام الكلي إلى الكلي، وتقييده به لا يستلزم الجزئية والتشخص، وإن كان قد يفيدها، فيكون حاصل الكلام أن المركبات العقلية مثل الجوهر المتميز والجسم النامي، والحيوان الناطق، والإنسان الفاضل، لا يلزم أن يكون جزئية، بل قد يكون كلية، وهذا من الوضوح بحيث لا ينبغي أن يخير به فضلا عن أن يجعل من المطالب العلمية.
It is necessary in determination that the concept be such that the mind cannot suppose it true of many, and this is the meaning of impossibility of sharing mentally. And it is known that this is not obtained by combining the universal with the universal, because each of the combined, the combined-to, and the combination, being universal, can be supposed by the mind to be true of many, indeed of infinite individuals, even if externally perhaps no individuals of it exist, but rather its multiplicity is impossible like the concept of the Necessary. If it is said: the judgment of the universal may differ from the judgment of each individual, so it is possible that each of the combined and the combined-to is universal, and the whole is particular. We say: there is no meaning for combination here except that the mind considers a universal concept like 'human', then considers for it a universal attribute like 'virtuous', and it is known necessarily that the universal described by universal attributes does not reach the level of particularity, even if that attribute is the concept of particularity, individuation, and impossibility of accepting sharing, the universality remains as it is. And it may be answered: that what is meant is that the combining of a universal to a universal, and its restriction by it, does not necessitate particularity and individuation, even if it may sometimes yield it. Thus the upshot is that mental composites such as 'discriminating substance', 'growing body', 'rational animal', and 'virtuous human' are not necessarily particular, but may be universal. And this is so obvious that it should not be used as a point of hesitation, let alone made one of the scientific inquiries.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation and defense of the earlier claim that combining universals does not produce a particular. The author reiterates the condition for determination: the concept must be such that the mind cannot suppose it true of many individuals – that is 'imtina' al-shirkah dhihnan'. He then explains why combining two universals fails: each component (the combined, the combined-to, and the act of combination) is itself universal, so the mind can still suppose the whole composite true of many individuals, even if in external reality only one individual exists (as with the concept of God). An objection is raised: perhaps the judgment of the whole differs from its parts; the parts are universal but the whole might be particular. The author responds by analyzing what 'combination' means here: it is merely the mind considering one universal concept and then adding another universal descriptor. The resulting composite remains universal, because describing a universal with another universal does not make it particular – even if the descriptor is 'particularity' itself. Then an alternative answer is given: the intended point is that combining universals does not entail particularity, though it might sometimes yield it in specific cases. The author illustrates with examples of mental composites (e.g., 'rational animal', 'virtuous human') that can still be predicated of many. He concludes that this is so obvious it should not be a matter of debate.

Key Terms
امتناع الشركة (imtina' al-shirkah)
الكلي (al-kullī)
الجزئي (al-juz'ī)
المركبات العقلية (al-murakkabāt al-'aqliyya)
الهذية (al-hudhiyya?)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a concept has only one external instance, it must be a particular concept.
Correction: A concept can be universal in the mind (sharable across many possible instances) even if only one instance exists externally, like the concept of the Necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the claim that combining two universals can yield a particular concept?
A) The combination is not a real mental operation.
B) Each component is universal, so the whole remains mentally shareable.
C) Universals cannot be combined at all.
D) The objection is false because the parts are not truly universal.

Key Takeaway
Combining two universals always yields a universal, not a particular, because each component and the combination itself are universally applicable in thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often define yourself by combining labels like 'Muslim', 'student', 'activist'—thinking these make you unique. But the author teaches that all such combinations remain universal. Your true uniqueness before Allah is not in these composites but in your individual soul that only Allah knows. How can you shift your focus from seeking identity in universals to seeking the particular relationship Allah wants with you?
?
The lesson says that even the concept of 'particularity' itself is universal. Have you ever believed that your trials or blessings are so uniquely yours that no one else can truly understand? Yet the concept of struggle itself is universal. How can this truth push you to seek solace in the community of believers and to see your experiences as a shared part of the human and Muslim condition?

4 min
The Definition of Ta'ayyun and Why Universals Cannot Produce It
The author defines particularization and proves it cannot result from combining universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a particular concept cannot be formed by combining universals because each component remains universal.

Core Idea
A particular concept must exclude all sharing; combining universals never achieves that.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid defines particularization as a concept that cannot apply to many. The author then argues that such particularity cannot arise from composing universals, no matter how we combine them. Understanding this logical point is crucial for distinguishing between universal and particular in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own phrasing come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the condition for particularization
The passage states that a particular (juz'i) must be a concept that the intellect cannot suppose to apply to many. This is the meaning of 'impossibility of sharing' conceptually. Read the first part carefully.
The term 'ta'ayyun' here refers to the act of becoming particular or the particular instance.
3
Understand why combining universals fails
The passage explains that combining universals cannot yield a particular because each element (the combined, the combined-to, and the act of combining) is itself universal and can be applied to many. See how the author breaks this down.
Even if external reality has only one individual, the concept itself remains universal.
4
Reflect on the example of the Necessary Being
The passage concludes with an example: the concept of the Necessary Being is universal even though its multiplicity is impossible in reality. This reinforces that particularity is not about existence but about conceptual applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد في التعين من كون المفهوم بحيث لا يمكن للعقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين، وهذا معنى امتناع الشركة ذهناً، ومعلوم أنه لا يحصل بانضمام الكلي إلى الكلي، لأن كلاً من المنضم والمنضم إليه والانضمام لكونه كلياً، يمكن للعقل فرض صدقه على كثيرين، بل على ما لا يتناهى من الأفراد، وإن كان بحسب الخارج ربما لا يوجد منه الأفراد، بل يمتنع تعدده كمفهوم الواجب.
In particularization (ta'ayyun), it is necessary that the concept be such that the intellect cannot suppose it to apply to many; this is the meaning of the impossibility of sharing (sharika) conceptually. It is known that this is not obtained by combining a universal with a universal, because each of the combined, the combined-to, and the combining, being universal, can be supposed by the intellect to apply to many, indeed to infinite individuals, although in external existence perhaps no individuals exist of it, or its multiplicity is impossible like the concept of the Necessary.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for particularization (ta'ayyun). The author states that for a concept to be a particular (juz'i), it must be such that the intellect cannot possibly apply it to more than one individual. This is the meaning of 'impossibility of sharing' conceptually. He then proves that this condition cannot be achieved by combining a universal with another universal, because each element of the combination—the subject universal, the predicate universal, and the act of combining—is itself universal and thus can be applied to many individuals. Even if in external reality only one individual exists (as with the concept of the Necessary Being), the concept itself remains universal. Therefore, particularity does not arise from such composition.

Key Terms
التعين (ta'ayyun)
الكلي (universal)
الجزئي (particular)
الشركة (sharika/sharing)
امتناع الشركة (impossibility of sharing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that combining two universals yields a particular concept.
Correction: The author clarifies that universality remains in each component and the combination, so the result is still universal.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the condition for a concept to be a particular (juz'i)?
A) It must be composed of universals.
B) The intellect cannot suppose it applies to many.
C) It must exist externally.
D) It must be infinite.

Key Takeaway
Particularity is the conceptual impossibility of sharing; it cannot be produced by combining universals.

?
The author says even the concept of the Necessary Being is universal in the mind. How does this shape your worship? Do you worship a concept, or the Real Existent beyond your conceptualization? Reflect on whether your devotion is to the actual Being of Allah or to a mental image.

Objection and Reply: Combining Universals
The author presents and refutes the objection that the rule of a universal may differ from its parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why combining two universal concepts does not necessarily produce a particular, and how the author refutes this objection.

Core Idea
Combining universals does not make them particular; universality persists.

This passage tackles a subtle logical objection: might the combination of two universals yield a particular? The author explains that in mental composites, the universality remains unless the combination itself introduces particularity, which it does not by its nature. We'll walk through the objection and the two layered replies.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection carefully: 'If it is said: The rule of a universal may differ...' Understand the claim that each part is universal but the sum could be particular.
Notice the objector is using the principle that the whole may have a different ruling than its parts.
2
Analyze the first reply
The first reply redefines 'combining' as the intellect considering a universal concept and then adding a universal attribute. Such a composite remains universal, as known by necessity.
Key phrase: 'الكلي الموصوف بالأوصاف الكلية لا ينتهي إلى حد الهذية'
3
Understand the second reply
The second reply clarifies that combining universals does not necessitate particularity, even if it sometimes yields it in external reality. The point is about mental composites.
This reply is a concession that combination may sometimes produce particularity, but it is not necessary.
4
Reflect on the summary
The author concludes that mental composites like 'rational animal' are not necessarily particular; they can be universal. This is obvious and should not be overcomplicated.
The author emphasizes the clarity of this point: 'لا ينبغي أن يخير به'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : حكم الكلي قد تخالف حكم كل واحد، فيجوز أن يكون كل من المنضم ، والمنضم إليه كلياً، والمجموع جزئياً. قلنا : لا معنى للانضمام ههنا سوى أن العقل يعتبر مفهوماً كلياً كالإنسان، ثم يعتبر له وصفاً كلياً كالفاضل ومعلوم بالضرورة أن الكلي الموصوف بالأوصاف الكلية لا ينتهي إلى حد الهذية، حتى لو كان ذلك الوصف هو مفهوم الجزئية والتشخص وامتناع قبول الشركة، كانت الكلية بحالها. وقد يجاب : بأن المراد أن انضمام الكلي إلى الكلي، وتقييده به لا يستلزم الجزئية والتشخص، وإن كان قد يفيدها، فيكون حاصل الكلام أن المركبات العقلية مثل الجوهر المتميز والجسم النامي، والحيوان الناطق، والإنسان الفاضل، لا يلزم أن يكون جزئية، بل قد يكون كلية، وهذا من الوضوح بحيث لا ينبغي أن يخير به فضلا عن أن يجعل من المطالب العلمية.
If it is said: The rule of a universal may differ from the rule of each part, so it is possible that each of the combined and the combined-to is universal, yet the sum is particular. We say: There is no meaning to 'combining' here except that the intellect considers a universal concept, like 'human', then considers for it a universal attribute, like 'virtuous'. It is known by necessity that a universal described by universal attributes does not reach the level of particularity (hudud al-hudhiyya); even if that attribute is the concept of particularity, individuation, and impossibility of accepting sharing, the universality remains as it was. And it may be answered: The intended meaning is that combining a universal with a universal and restricting it by it does not necessitate particularity and individuation, even if it may sometimes yield it. So the summary is that mental composites like 'distinct substance', 'growing body', 'rational animal', and 'virtuous human' are not necessarily particular; rather they may be universal. This is so obvious that one should not be confused by it, let alone make it a scientific inquiry.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and two replies. The objector argues that the rule of a universal may differ from the rule of its parts, so it is possible that each component is universal while the composite is particular. The first reply denies that 'combining' in this context means anything more than the intellect considering a universal concept and then adding a universal attribute; such a composite remains universal. The second reply clarifies that combining universals does not entail particularity, even if it sometimes produces particularity in external reality. The author emphasizes that mental composites like 'rational animal' are not necessarily particular; the point is obvious and should not be treated as a scientific problem.

Key Terms
الكلي (universal)
الجزئي (particular)
الانضمام (combining)
التشخص (individuation)
المركبات العقلية (mental composites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that combining two universal concepts always yields a particular (e.g., 'virtuous human' is particular).
Correction: The author shows that in mental composites, the universality remains; 'virtuous human' can be understood as a universal concept, not necessarily a specific individual.

Checkpoint
According to the author's first reply, what happens when you describe a universal concept with a universal attribute?
A) It becomes a particular.
B) It remains universal.
C) It ceases to exist.
D) It becomes a necessary being.

Key Takeaway
Mental composites like 'rational animal' are not necessarily particular; they remain universal, and this logical point is too obvious to be a scientific problem.

Objection and Reply: Infinite Regress of Particularization
Objection-and-reply on infinite regress of particularization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni theologians rebut the objection that combining universals leads to infinite regress by grounding particularity in external existence and divine creation.

Core Idea
Particularity originates from the external individual, not from conceptual combination.

This lesson presents a classic objection against the Sunni position on universals and particularity. The objector argues that if combining a universal with something else makes it particular, then that something else itself must be particularized, leading to an infinite chain. The author replies by clarifying that universals exist only in the mind; actual individuals are directly created by God. This refutes the regress by shifting the basis of particularity from mental composition to external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully from the Arabic and translation. Note the argument that particularization requires an infinite series of combined universals.
The objection assumes that combining universals is the only way to produce particularity.
2
Identify the regress
Understand why the objector thinks infinite regress follows: each combiner must itself be particular, requiring another combiner.
This is a logical problem for any theory that bases particularity on composition.
3
Analyze the reply
Read the author's reply. Notice the key distinction: universals are mental abstractions; external existents are individuals. No external 'universal' exists to combine.
The intellect extracts universal forms from individuals, not the other way around.
4
Compare two views
Note the two accounts of how individuals become particular: for Sunnis, by divine creation; for others, by the realization of quiddity in the external world.
Both avoid infinite regress by locating particularity outside the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فعلى ما ذكرتم يلزم أن يكون ما ينضم إلى الكلى، وتقيده الجزئية جزئياً، وله لا محالة مفهوم كلي يفتقر إلى ما ينضم إليه، ويجعله جزئياً ويتسلسل . قلنا : ليس هناك موجود هو الكلي، وآخر ينضم إليه ويجعله جزئياً، بل الموجود الأشخاص، والعقل ينتزع منها الصور الكلية بحسب الاستعدادات والاعتبارات المختلفة، والمقصود أن المعنى الذي يسببه امتنع للعقل فرض صدق المفهوم على الكثيرين لا يصلح أن يكون انضمام الكلي إلى الكلي، بل الشخص يستند عندنا إلى القادر المختار كسائر الممكنات بمعنى أنه الموجد لكل فرد على ما شاء من التشخص، وعند بعضهم إلى تحقق الماهية في الخارج، للقطع بأنها إذا تحققت لم يكن إلا فرداً مخصوصاً لا تعدد فيه ولا اشتراك، وإنما قبول التعدد والاشتراك في المفهوم الحاصل في العقل .
If it is said: According to what you have mentioned, it follows that what combines with the universal and restricts it to particularity is itself particular, and it necessarily has a universal concept that requires something to combine with it and make it particular, leading to infinite regress. We say: There is no existent that is the universal and another that combines with it and makes it particular; rather existents are individuals, and the intellect extracts from them universal forms according to different dispositions and considerations. The intended meaning is that the meaning which causes the intellect to be unable to suppose the concept applies to many cannot be the combining of a universal with a universal. Rather, the individual, according to us (the Sunni theologians), is dependent on the Omnipotent Chooser (al-Qadir al-Mukhtar) like all other contingents, meaning that He is the creator of each individual according to His will of individuation. According to some others, it is dependent on the realization of the quiddity in external existence, because it is certain that when it is realized, it is only a particular individual with no multiplicity or sharing; multiplicity and sharing only occur in the conceptual form obtained in the intellect.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection about infinite regress. The objector argues that if the principle that combining universals does not yield particularity is accepted, then the element that combines with the universal to make it particular would itself need to be particularized by another element, leading to an infinite chain. The reply distinguishes between the mental and the external: there is no external universal that combines with another; rather, external things are individuals, and the intellect abstracts universals from them. The real basis of particularity is not conceptual composition but either divine creation (for the Sunnis) or the realization of the quiddity in external existence (for others). Thus, no infinite regress occurs because particularity originates from the external individual, not from mental composition.

Key Terms
التسلسل (infinite regress)
القادر المختار (Omnipotent Chooser)
الماهية (quiddity)
التشخص (individuation)
الأشخاص (individuals)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that particularity necessarily requires an infinite chain of combinations, making the concept of an individual impossible without regress.
Correction: The author shows that particularity is not based on mental combination but on external factors: either divine creation or the realization of quiddity in the external world, thus no regress occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the source of particularity for Sunni theologians?
A) Combining universals.
B) The Omnipotent Chooser (al-Qadir al-Mukhtar).
C) The intellect's abstraction.
D) The universal itself.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress of particularization is avoided by recognizing that universals are mental abstractions; actual individuals are directly brought into existence by God or through the realization of quiddity in the external world.

How would your daily gratitude change if you truly internalized that there is no universal 'you' out there, only God's direct creation of your individual self?
?

Unity of Existence and Multiplicity of Particulars
Objection and reply on unity of existence and multiplicity of particulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the conceptual unity of existence is compatible with the multiplicity of particular individuals according to Islamic kalam.

Core Idea
Conceptual unity does not prevent real multiplicity.

This short passage addresses a key objection against the unity of existence: if existence is one, how can there be many different things? The author clarifies that existence is one in meaning, but its instances are many due to differentiating factors. This distinction preserves both unity and multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening objection carefully: 'If it is said: It follows that particularization would not be multiple, because existence is one thing.' Notice the logical inference being challenged.
The objection equates conceptual unity with concrete singularity.
2
Study the reply
Read the reply: 'We say: Even if it is one in terms of concept, its individuals are multiple according to times, places, materials, and other causes.' Observe the distinction introduced.
The author uses 'afrad' (individuals) to denote concrete instantiations.
3
Identify the key distinction
Recognize that the reply separates conceptual unity (wahdat al-mutashabih) from concrete multiplicity. Existence as an abstract concept is one, but its instances are many.
This is a classic move in kalam to reconcile unity with diversity.
4
Connect to external causes
Note the four factors mentioned: times, places, materials, and other causes. These external conditions individuate existence into multiple particulars.
The list is not exhaustive; 'other causes' covers additional differentiators.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيلزم أن لا يتعدد التعين، لأن الوجود أمر واحد . قلنا : هو وإن كان واحداً بحسب المفهوم، لكن يتعدد أفراده بحسب الأزمنة والأمكنة والمواد وسائر الأسباب، فتتعدد التعينات .
If it is said: It follows that particularization (ta'ayyun) would not be multiple, because existence is one thing. We say: Even if it is one in terms of concept, its individuals are multiple according to times, places, materials, and other causes; thus particularizations become multiple.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that if existence is one, then particularization cannot be multiple. The reply clarifies that existence is one in its conceptual meaning, but its instances are many due to external factors like time, place, matter, and other causes. Therefore, particularizations—the individuations that distinguish one existent from another—are multiple. This preserves the multiplicity of concrete individuals despite the conceptual unity of existence.

Key Terms
الوجود (existence)
التعين (particularization)
الأفراد (individuals)
الأزمنة (times)
الأمكنة (places)
المواد (materials)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is one, then all particulars must be one.
Correction: Existence is one in meaning, but its instances are many due to external factors such as time, place, material, and other causes.

Checkpoint

A) Existence is not really one.
B) Individuals differ by times, places, materials, and other causes.
C) Existence is multiple in concept.
D) Particularization is not real.

Key Takeaway
The conceptual unity of existence does not negate the multiplicity of particularized existents due to differentiating causes.

Refuting the Infinite Regress in Universal-Particular Combination
Refutation of the objection that particularization leads to infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the particularization of a universal does not lead to infinite regress because universals are mental abstractions, not external entities.

Core Idea
Universals are not real things added to things; they are mental abstractions from individuals.

The objection of infinite regress is a classic challenge to any theory that tries to explain how universals become particular. In this passage, the author refutes the objection by denying that there is any external universal entity that needs to be combined with another. Instead, only individual things exist externally, and universals are concepts the mind abstracts from them. This distinction resolves the regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part ('فإن قيل...') which presents the opponent's argument that particularization leads to infinite regress. Notice the logical chain: if a universal becomes particular by adding something, that something must itself be a particular, requiring another addition, ad infinitum.
This objection assumes universals are real entities that can be added together.
2
Understand the author's response
Read the answer starting with 'قلنا...'. The author denies the premise that there are separate universal existents. Instead, only individuals exist; universals are mental abstractions. The particularization of an individual is not a combination of universals but a direct act of God or the realization of essence.
The key move is refuting the existence of universals as external entities.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view preserves God's direct creative power and avoids the philosophical problems of Platonic forms. Also note that the mind abstracts universals from shared features of individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : - فعلى ما ذكرتم يلزم أن يكون ما ينضم إلى الكلى، وتقيده الجزئية جزئياً، وله لا محالة مفهوم كلي يفتقر إلى ما ينضم إليه، ويجعله جزئياً ويتسلسل . قلنا : ليس هناك موجود هو الكلي، وآخر ينضم إليه ويجعله جزئياً، بل الموجود الأشخاص، والعقل ينتزع منها الصور الكلية بحسب الاستعدادات والاعتبارات المختلفة، والمقصود أن المعنى الذي يسببه امتنع للعقل فرض صدق المفهوم على الكثيرين لا يصلح أن يكون انضمام الكلي إلى الكلي، بل الشخص يستند عندنا إلى القادر المختار كسائر الممكنات بمعنى أنه الموجد لكل فرد على ما شاء من التشخص، وعند بعضهم إلى تحقق الماهية في الخارج، للقطع بأنها إذا تحققت لم يكن إلا فرداً مخصوصاً لا تعدد فيه ولا اشتراك، وإنما قبول التعدد والاشتراك في المفهوم الحاصل في العقل .
If it is said: according to what you have mentioned, it follows that what is joined to the universal, and by which it becomes particular, is itself particular, and it inevitably has a universal concept which needs something to join to it and make it particular, leading to infinite regress. We say: There is no existent that is the universal and another that is joined to it and makes it particular. Rather, the existents are the individuals, and the mind abstracts from them the universal forms according to different dispositions and considerations. The point is that the meaning which prevents the mind from supposing the concept to apply to many cannot be the joining of a universal to a universal. Rather, the individual (al-shakhs) is dependent, according to us, upon the Omnipotent Chooser like all other possibles, meaning that He is the originator of every individual as He wills of particularization; and according to some, upon the realization of the essence (al-mahiyya) in the external world, because it is certain that when it is realized, it is nothing but a specific individual without multiplicity or sharing. Rather, the acceptance of multiplicity and sharing is in the concept (al-mafhum) obtained in the mind.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that if every particular is formed by adding something to a universal, then that added thing must itself be a particular, requiring another universal to particularize it, leading to an infinite regress. The author (al-Taftazani) replies by denying the premise that there exists a universal as a separate external entity that is added to another. Instead, only individual entities exist externally. The mind abstracts universal concepts from these individuals based on shared attributes. Thus, the particularization of an individual is not a process of adding one universal to another. According to Sunni theologians, the individual's existence and particularization are directly from God; according to others, it is from the realization of the essence in the external world, which necessarily yields a unique individual. Universality and multiplicity exist only in the mind's concepts, not externally.

Key Terms
كلي (kulli, universal)
جزئي (juz'i, particular)
تشخص (tashakhkhus, particularization)
مفهوم (mafhum, concept)
ماهية (mahiyya, essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The universal is a real entity that can be added to another to create a particular in the external world.
Correction: The author argues that universals are not external entities but mental abstractions from individuals. Only individuals exist externally; particularization is not a combination of universals.

Checkpoint
According to the author, where do universal concepts come from?
A) They exist as separate Platonic forms.
B) They are created directly by God.
C) They are abstracted by the mind from individuals.
D) They are identical with essences in the external world.

Key Takeaway
The particularization of a universal does not lead to infinite regress because universals are mental abstractions from externally existing individuals, and God directly creates each individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about concepts like 'justice' or 'truth', do you unconsciously treat them as if they were real things existing somewhere outside your mind? How does this lesson's distinction between mental universals and external individuals challenge that habit?

Does your daily interaction with others reflect that belief, or do you tend to categorize and label them with universal concepts, forgetting their unique particularity?
?
How often do you catch yourself arguing about abstract ideas as if they were tangible objects, when in fact they are just mental constructs? Could this intellectual habit be obscuring a more direct reliance on Allah, who alone creates and sustains every individual?

Multiplicity of Particularizations Despite the Conceptual Unity of Existence
Answer to the objection that particularization cannot be multiple given the unity of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the unity of existence in concept does not necessitate a single particularization, because the individuals of existence are conditioned by diverse factors.

Core Idea
Existence is one in concept but many in reality, allowing multiple particularizations.

A common objection in kalam is that if existence is one, then particularization (the determination that makes each existent unique) must also be one. But this confuses conceptual unity with real multiplicity. In this passage, the author clarifies that existence, though one in our mental definition, manifests in many individuals shaped by different times, places, and causes. The response shows how conceptual simplicity and real diversity can coexist.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: 'If it is said... We say...' Identify the objector's claim and the author's reply.
The objector argues that from the unity of existence, particularization must be one.
2
Understand the key distinction
Focus on the author's move: existence is one 'according to the concept' (bi hasab al-mafhum) but its individuals are multiple. This is the core of the answer.
The concept of existence is a single mental notion, but in the external world, each existing thing is a distinct individual.
3
Identify the factors that differentiate individuals
The author lists times, places, matters, and other causes as the sources of multiplicity. Consider how these factors condition each instance of existence.
These are the 'conditions of individuation' that make each existent unique.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this matters: if existence were one in reality (like pantheism), then particularization would be one. But classical Sunni theology holds that existence is multiple in reality, so particularizations are multiple as well.
This preserves the multiplicity of created beings and avoids monism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فيلزم أن لا يتعدد التعين، لأن الوجود أمر واحد . قلنا : هو وإن كان واحداً بحسب المفهوم، لكن يتعدد أفراده بحسب الأزمنة والأمكنة والمواد وسائر الأسباب، فتتعدد التعينات .
If it is said: then it follows that particularization (al-ta'ayyun) cannot be multiple, because existence (al-wujud) is one thing. We say: Even if it is one in concept, its individuals are multiple according to times, places, matters, and all other causes, so particularizations become multiple.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that since existence is one (either in the sense of the concept of existence being univocal or in the sense of a singular reality), particularization must also be one. The author replies that existence is one only in the mind as a concept. In external reality, the individual instances of existence are many, each conditioned by different times, places, materials, and other causes. Therefore, the particularizations (the specific determinations that make each individual this individual) are likewise multiple, corresponding to the multiplicity of existent individuals.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun, particularization/determination)
وجود (wujud, existence)
مفهوم (mafhum, concept)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unity of existence in the mind implies unity in external reality.
Correction: Existence is one only as a concept; its individuals are many due to different conditioning factors.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the multiplicity of particularizations despite the conceptual unity of existence?
A) By denying the unity of existence.
B) By stating that existence has multiple forms in the mind.
C) By pointing to the different conditions that individuate each instance of existence.
D) By saying that particularizations are illusions.

Key Takeaway
Existence is one conceptually but multiple in reality, so particularizations are also multiple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'God is One.' Have you mistaken that to mean that all creation is one in the same way, blurring the distinction between Creator and created? ?
Think of a time when you treated an abstract concept—like 'justice' or 'truth'—as if it were a single, uniform reality, ignoring the different ways it manifests in different situations. How did that lead to a mistake in judgment or action? What will you do next time you catch yourself conflating concept with concrete reality?

Possibility is Essential, Not Accidental
The commentator defines predication and argues that possibility is essential, rejecting accidental possibility via the impossibility of reversal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility (imkan) must be an essential attribute (dhati) and not accidental (ghayri), and how the concept of reversal (inqlab) refutes accidental possibility.

Core Idea
Possibility is essential—if accidental, it would require the impossible reversal of a thing's essence.

This lesson delves into a foundational distinction in kalam: the nature of possibility. The commentator explains what it means to predicate something of a subject and then argues that possibility cannot be a mere accidental property; it must be an essential attribute of the possible thing. Understanding this argument helps you grasp a key logical principle underlying discussions of contingency and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation to get an overall sense of the argument.
Pay attention to the opening clarification about predication.
2
Understand predication clarification
The commentator clarifies that saying 'man is a writer' means truth and predication, not existence as a writer. Notice the equivalence of terms like 'exists for him' and 'is true of'.
This sets the stage for discussing attributes.
3
Examine the argument against accidental possibility
Focus on the conditional reasoning: if possibility were accidental, a thing would first be necessary or impossible by essence, then become possible by another—this removes the essential attribute, which is impossible.
The concept of 'inqlab' (reversal) is introduced as the name for this impossibility.
4
Reflect on the logical necessity
Consider why the argument is considered 'impossible by necessity'. This shows that possibility must be an essential attribute, not added from outside.
This connects to the broader kalam project of classifying things into necessary, impossible, and possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإنسان كاتب، ويمتنع أن يكون معناه أنه يوجد كاتباً أو توجد له الكتابة : بل معناه أن ما صدق عليه هذا يصدق عليه ذاك أو يحمل .
والمحققون على أنه لا فرق بين قولنا : يوجد له ذاك، ويثبت، ويصدق عليه، ويحمل ونحو ذلك إلا بحسب العبارة، وما ذكرنا هو الموافق لكلام المحقق في التجريد.
قال والإمكان ذاتي لا غير .
إذ لو كان غيرياً لكان الشيء في نفسه واجباً أو ممتنعاً، أي ضروري الوجود أو العدم بالذات ثم يصير لا ضروري الوجود والعدم بالغير، فيرتفع ما بالذات، وهو محال بالضرورة، وهذا معنى الانقلاب.
The human is a writer, and it is impossible that its meaning be that he exists as a writer or that writing exists for him; rather its meaning is that what is true of this is true of that or is predicated.
And the verifiers hold that there is no difference between our saying: 'exists for him that,' and 'is established,' and 'is true of,' and 'is predicated,' and the like, except in terms of expression. And what we have mentioned is what agrees with the statement of the verifier in al-Tajrid.
He said: 'And possibility is essential, not otherwise.'
For if it were accidental, then the thing in itself would be necessary or impossible, i.e., necessary of existence or non-existence by essence, then it becomes not necessary of existence and non-existence by another, so what is by essence is removed, and that is impossible by necessity. And this is the meaning of reversal.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of possibility (imkan) and argues that it is an essential attribute (dhati) rather than accidental (ghayri). It begins by clarifying that saying 'man is a writer' does not mean that he exists as a writer or that writing exists for him, but rather that the subject and predicate correspond truthfully (sidq) or that the predicate is predicated on the subject. The commentator then notes that the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) hold that expressions like 'exists for him,' 'is established,' 'is true of,' and 'is predicated' are equivalent in meaning, differing only in phrasing. This matches the position of the verifier in al-Tajrid. Then the matn statement is quoted: 'And possibility is essential, not otherwise.' The commentator explains that if possibility were accidental (ghayri), it would imply that a thing is necessary or impossible in itself (by essence) but then becomes non-necessary by something else, which would require the removal of an essential attribute—an impossibility. This impossible situation is called 'inqlab' (reversal). Thus, possibility must be an essential attribute.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkan)
ذاتي (dhati)
غيري (ghayri)
انقلاب (inqlab)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is an accidental attribute that can be added or removed from a thing.
Correction: The passage refutes this by showing that if possibility were accidental, it would require removing an essential attribute (necessity or impossibility), which is impossible. Hence possibility must be essential.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that possibility cannot be accidental (ghayri)?
A) Because accidental attributes cannot change
B) Because it would require a thing to change from necessary to non-necessary by another, removing its essential attribute
C) Because possibility only applies to contingents
D) Because the verifiers have decided it is essential

Key Takeaway
Possibility is an essential attribute of a contingent thing, not an accidental one; this is proven by the impossibility of reversal (inqlab).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If possibility is essential to everything contingent, then your very existence is not a matter of accident but of essence. Does this make you feel fragile or anchored? How does this truth drive you to seek the One who is Necessary by Essence?
?
The argument shows that an essential attribute cannot be removed. Think of a time you thought a character flaw could be easily changed by circumstances. How does this lesson reveal the danger of treating your own sins as accidental rather than as a deep rebellion that requires a transformation of the heart?
?
Predication is about truth correspondence, not about attributes inhering. In your life, do you often mistake the outward appearance of faith for the inward truth? When you say 'I am a believer', does that correspond to reality, or have you attached the label without the essence?

Correcting the Meaning of General Possibility
Correction of the concept of general possibility and resolution of a logical puzzle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of general possibility (al-imkan al-amm) and how it resolves a logical puzzle about the negation of general and specific terms.

Core Idea
General possibility is not a single concept but differs for existence and non-existence.

Many students of kalam mistakenly think general possibility has one definition that covers all types. In this passage, the author corrects this misunderstanding and shows how getting it right solves a tricky logical problem. Let's read the text carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Identify the erroneous view that many people hold about general possibility. Note the author's immediate rejection of it.
2
Understand the correction
The author explains that possibility of existence means negation of impossibility, and possibility of non-existence means negation of necessity. So general possibility is not a single concept.
3
See how the puzzle is resolved
The logical puzzle about the negation of general and specific terms is resolved by applying the corrected understanding. Follow the author's reasoning step by step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي امتناع العدم. وقد سبق إلى كثير من الأوهام، أن للإمكان العام مفهوماً واحداً، يعم الإمكان الخاص والوجوب والامتناع هو سلب ضرورة أحد الطرفين، أعني الوجود والعدم وهو بعيد جداً. إذ لا يفهم هذا المعنى من إمكان الشيء على الإطلاق، بل إنما يفهم من إمكان وجوده نفي الامتناع، ومن إمكان عدمه نفي الوجوب. ولهذا يقع الممكن العام مقابلا للممتنع شاملاً للواجب كما في تقسيم الكلي إلى الممتنع، وإلى الممكن الذي أحد أقسامه أن يوجد منه فرد واحد مع امتناع غيره، كالواجب، وبهذا ينحل ما يقال على قاعدة كون نقيض الأعم أخص من نقيض الأخص، من هذا أنه لو صح الصدق في قولنا كل ما ليس بممكن عام ليس بممكن خاص لكنه باطل لأن كل ما ليس بممكن خاص، فهو إما واجب أو ممتنع، وكل منهما ممكن عام، فيلزم أن كل ما ليس بممكن عام فهو ممكن عام .
Negation of the impossibility of non-existence. Many have erroneously thought that general possibility has a single concept encompassing specific possibility, necessity, and impossibility, namely the negation of the necessity of one of the two sides, i.e., existence and non-existence. This is far off. For this meaning is not understood from the possibility of a thing absolutely, but rather from the possibility of its existence one understands negation of impossibility, and from the possibility of its non-existence one understands negation of necessity. For this reason, general possibility is contrasted with the impossible and includes the necessary, as in the division of the universal into the impossible and the possible, one of whose divisions is that a single individual exists from it while others are impossible, like the necessary. And by this is resolved what is said based on the rule that the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific: that if the statement 'Everything that is not generally possible is not specifically possible' were true, then it would be false because everything that is not specifically possible is either necessary or impossible, and both are generally possible. So it would follow that everything that is not generally possible is generally possible.
Source pages p. 462

Commentary
This passage corrects a common misunderstanding about the meaning of general possibility (al-imkan al-amm). The author first states the erroneous view: that general possibility has a single meaning, namely the negation of the necessity of either existence or non-existence. He refutes this, saying that this meaning is not what is usually understood from possibility; rather, possibility of existence means negation of impossibility, and possibility of non-existence means negation of necessity. Therefore, general possibility is contrasted with the impossible and includes the necessary (as in the case of the necessary being, which has a specific kind of possibility). The author then shows how this correct understanding resolves a logical puzzle based on the rule that the negation of a broader term is more specific than the negation of a narrower term. The puzzle claimed that if we say 'everything that is not generally possible is not specifically possible', it would be false because what is not specifically possible is either necessary or impossible, both of which are generally possible, leading to a contradiction. The resolution lies in distinguishing the different senses of possibility: general possibility applies to necessary and impossible in different respects, not under a single concept.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
الممتنع
الواجب
نقيض الأعم
نقيض الأخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General possibility has one concept encompassing specific possibility, necessity, and impossibility.
Correction: General possibility differs based on whether it is possibility of existence (negation of impossibility) or possibility of non-existence (negation of necessity).

Checkpoint
What is the correct understanding of general possibility according to this passage?
A) It is the negation of the necessity of both existence and non-existence.
B) It is a single concept encompassing all types of possibility.
C) It refers to the negation of impossibility in the possibility of existence and negation of necessity in the possibility of non-existence.
D) It is synonymous with specific possibility.

Key Takeaway
General possibility must be understood in two senses to avoid logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have I ever assumed a single definition for a concept in my faith that actually has multiple meanings, leading to confusion or error in my understanding? Am I willing to examine my beliefs with this level of logical precision, even when it is uncomfortable?
?
When I encounter a logical contradiction in religious matters, do I dismiss it intellectually or take the time to clarify the meanings of the terms involved, as this lesson requires? What is at stake if I don't?
?
How often do I settle for simplistic, surface-level understandings of complex theological concepts to avoid mental effort? Will I commit to doing the hard work of resolving apparent contradictions, starting with the concept of general possibility in my own mind?

Preparatory Possibility: Definition and Explanation
Definition of preparatory possibility as matter's preparedness for forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of preparatory possibility and how it differs from intrinsic possibility.

Core Idea
Preparatory possibility is conditional, not essential—it depends on causes and conditions.

The author now introduces a second kind of possibility: preparatory possibility. Unlike intrinsic possibility, which is essential and unchanging, this type varies in strength and arises only when matter is prepared to receive a form. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing how things come into existence in the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author defines preparatory possibility and gives examples.
Pay attention to the contrast with intrinsic possibility.
2
Identify the key characteristics
Look for how the author describes the variation in strength and weakness, and the condition that it does not reach the limit of necessity.
3
Examine the examples
Trace the examples of the sperm and the child learning to write. How do they illustrate the concept?
These examples show progressive preparedness.
4
Understand the comparison
Focus on the final sentence where the author contrasts preparatory possibility with intrinsic possibility.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد يعتبر بمعنى تهيؤ المادة لحصول الشيء، باعتبار تحقق الشرائط، فتتفاوت شدة وضعفاً وتسمى استعدادية. إشارة إلى الإمكان الاستعدادي، وهو تهيؤ المادة لما يحصل لها من الصور والأعراض بتحقق بعض الأسباب والشرائط بحيث لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب الحاصل عند تمام العلة، ويتفاوت شدة وضعفاً بحسب القرب من الحصول أو البعد عنه بناء على حصول الكثير مما لا بد منه، والقليل كاستعداد الإنسانية الحاصل للنطفة ثم للعلقة، ثم للمضغة، وكاستعداد الكتابة الحاصل للجنين ثم للطفل، وهكذا إلى أن يتعلم، وهذا الإمكان ليس لازماً للماهية كالإمكان الذاتي، بل يوجد بعد العدم، بحدوث بعض الأسباب والشرائط، وبعدم بعد الوجود الحصول الشيء بالفعل.
He said: And it may be considered in the sense of the preparedness (tahayyu') of matter for the acquisition of a thing, with consideration of the fulfillment of conditions. It varies in strength and weakness and is called 'preparatory possibility'. This refers to preparatory possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi), which is the preparedness of matter for the forms and accidents that occur to it by the realization of some causes and conditions, such that it does not reach the limit of necessity that occurs when the cause is complete. It varies in strength and weakness according to proximity to or distance from acquisition, based on the occurrence of much or little of what is necessary. Like the preparedness for humanity that occurs in the sperm, then the clot, then the embryo, and like the preparedness for writing that occurs in the fetus, then the child, and so on until he learns. This possibility is not necessary for the essence like intrinsic possibility (al-imkan al-dhati), but rather occurs after non-existence by the occurrence of some causes and conditions, and by the absence of the acquisition of the thing in actuality.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage defines preparatory possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi), which is different from intrinsic possibility (al-imkan al-dhati). It is the preparedness (tahayyu') of a material substrate to receive a form or accident when certain conditions are met. The author explains that it varies in intensity and weakness depending on how close or far the thing is from actualization, and it does not reach the point of necessity that comes with a complete cause. Examples are given: the preparedness for humanity in the sperm, clot, and embryo, and the preparedness for writing in the developing child. This type of possibility is not intrinsic to the essence but rather arises after non-existence due to the occurrence of some causes and conditions, and it disappears once the thing is actually acquired.

Key Terms
الإمكان الاستعدادي
تهيؤ المادة
الإمكان الذاتي
العلة
القرب والبعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking preparatory possibility is the same as intrinsic possibility or that it is essential to the thing.
Correction: Preparatory possibility is conditional and arises only when matter is prepared by causes and conditions; it is not an essential attribute of the thing.

Checkpoint
How does preparatory possibility differ from intrinsic possibility?
A) It is necessary for the essence.
B) It arises after non-existence due to causes and conditions.
C) It is the same as intrinsic possibility.
D) It is only found in necessary beings.

Key Takeaway
Preparatory possibility is the conditional preparedness of matter for a form, varying in strength and ceasing upon actualization, unlike intrinsic possibility which is essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that preparatory possibility varies in strength based on how close you are to actualizing something. Think of a spiritual goal you are working toward—like praying on time or memorizing Quran. What conditions have you established to increase your preparedness? Which condition are you neglecting, and how will you address it today?
?
The passage says preparatory possibility ceases once the thing is actually acquired. Have you ever achieved a spiritual state (like humility in prayer) only to lose it because you stopped the conditions that brought it about? What is one condition you can recommit to right now so that you don't regress?

Future Possibility and the Avoidance of Contradiction
Refutes a misinterpretation of possibility requiring simultaneous existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of future possibility (al-imkan al-istiqbali) resolves an apparent contradiction in the definition of possibility as applying to both existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Possibility doesn't mean both at once; it means future contingency.

In this passage, the author addresses a confusion that might arise from the standard definition of the possible (al-mumkin) as that which can both exist and not exist. A critic might argue that this implies a simultaneous contradiction. The author clarifies by introducing the idea of future possibility, showing how the definition is logically sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its argument. Then read the literal translation to understand the logical steps.
Notice the technical terms: إمكان, وجود, عدم.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the objector's reasoning is stated: that possibility in both sides would require absence of existence and absence of non-existence, leading to a contradiction.
The objection is in the first few lines.
3
See the author's clarification
Focus on the author's response: the intended meaning is future possibility (الإمكان الاستقبالي), which only entails possibility of non-occurrence, not occurrence of non-existence.
This avoids requiring simultaneous presence and absence.
4
Understand the logical resolution
Consider how the author shows that if we instead consider future possibility regarding non-existence, then existence is required in the present, which is not contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن ممكن في جانبي الوجود والعدم، وكما أن الوجود يخرجه إلى جانب الوجود كذلك، ويشترط الخلو عنه. كذلك العدم يخرجه إلى جانب الامتناع، فيلزم اشتراط الخلو عنه أيضاً، فيلزم ارتفاع النقيضين بل اجتماعهما، والظاهر من اشترط ذلك أراد بالإمكان الاستقبالي إمكان حدوث الوجود، وطريانه في المستقبل وهو إنما يستلزم إمكان عدم الحدوث، لا إمكان حدوث العدم، ليلزم اشتراط الوجود في الحال، بل لو اعتبر الإمكان الاستقبالي في جانب العدم، بمعنى إمكان طريان العدم وحدوثه، يشترط الوجود في الحال من غير لزوم محال.
That something possible in both sides of existence and non-existence, and just as existence brings it out to the side of existence, likewise, absence is required from it. Likewise, non-existence brings it out to the side of impossibility, so the requirement of absence is also entailed, so the raising of the two contradictories, or rather their conjunction, is entailed. And it appears that the one who stipulated that intended by future possibility the possibility of the occurrence of existence, and its occurring in the future, and it only entails the possibility of non-occurrence, not the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence, so that the requirement of existence in the present is entailed. Rather, if future possibility is considered in the side of non-existence, meaning the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence and its happening, then existence is required in the present without entailing an impossibility.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection regarding the nature of possibility. The objector claims that if a thing is possible in both aspects of existence and non-existence, then existence would require absence of existence, and non-existence would require absence of non-existence, leading to either raising both contradictories (i.e., neither existence nor non-existence) or their conjunction. The author clarifies that the objector has misunderstood. The intended meaning is future possibility (al-imkan al-istiqbali): the possibility of the occurrence of existence in the future. This only entails the possibility of non-occurrence, not the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence. Hence, there is no requirement for existence to be absent in the present; rather, existence is required for non-existence to occur later. If future possibility is considered regarding non-existence, then existence is required in the present without any impossibility. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
إمكان
استقبالي
وجود
عدم
نقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'possible in both sides' means the thing must simultaneously exist and not exist.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it means future contingency: the thing can come into existence or cease to exist, not that both states hold at once.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'future possibility' (al-imkan al-istiqbali) entail regarding existence?
A) It entails the possibility of the occurrence of non-existence.
B) It entails the possibility of non-occurrence of existence.
C) It entails that existence and non-existence are both present.
D) It entails that existence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The definition of possibility as applying to both existence and non-existence refers to future contingency, avoiding any contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you sometimes hide behind the idea that 'anything is possible' in a way that paralyzes you? How does this lesson's distinction between vague possibility and genuine future contingency call you to face the actual choices before you?
?
The passage shows that for a future possibility to be real, the present must be definite. Is there an area of your life where you are avoiding making a present commitment by claiming 'it could go either way'? What would it mean to live today with the clarity that something is either going to happen or not?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's decree includes future possibilities without contradiction, how does that reshape your trust in His plan when you are uncertain about what will happen?

Preparedness Possibility: The Dynamic Potential
Defines al-imkan al-isti'dadi and illustrates it with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of preparedness possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi), how it differs from essential possibility, and why it explains gradual change in creation.

Core Idea
Preparedness possibility is the potential that grows nearer to actuality step by step.

Having understood essential possibility as a neutral potential inherent in things, we now encounter a more dynamic type: preparedness possibility. This is the readiness of matter to receive forms and accidents as conditions are met, but without reaching necessity. Sanusi explains this with vivid examples, showing how the same entity can be more or less prepared to become something. The passage clarifies that this possibility is temporary and depends on external causes.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Read Sanusi's definition of preparedness possibility and note how it is tied to matter and conditions. It is the preparedness of matter to acquire a thing upon realization of some conditions, not reaching the necessity of a complete cause.
Notice that it does not become necessary until all causes are complete.
2
Follow the examples
The sperm-drop example shows stages from drop to clot to chewed lump, each stage having a different intensity of preparedness for humanity. The writing example shows the fetus then child gradually prepared to learn.
These examples illustrate how preparedness varies in intensity and weakness.
3
Distinguish from essential possibility
Contrast this with essential possibility: preparedness possibility is not necessary for the quiddity, it arises after non-existence due to causes and ceases after actual existence.
This distinction is key to understanding why some potentials are temporary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وقد يعتبر بمعنى تهيؤ المادة لحصول الشيء، باعتبار تحقق الشرائط، فتتفاوت شدة وضعفاً وتسمى استعدادية. إشارة إلى الإمكان الاستعدادي، وهو تهيؤ المادة لما يحصل لها من الصور والأعراض بتحقق بعض الأسباب والشرائط بحيث لا ينتهي إلى حد الوجوب الحاصل عند تمام العلة، ويتفاوت شدة وضعفاً بحسب القرب من الحصول أو البعد عنه بناء على حصول الكثير مما لا بد منه، والقليل كاستعداد الإنسانية الحاصل للنطفة ثم للعلقة، ثم للمضغة، وكاستعداد الكتابة الحاصل للجنين ثم للطفل، وهكذا إلى أن يتعلم، وهذا الإمكان ليس لازماً للماهية كالإمكان الذاتي، بل يوجد بعد العدم، بحدوث بعض الأسباب والشرائط، وبعدم بعد الوجود الحصول الشيء بالفعل.
He said: And it may be considered in the sense of the preparedness of matter for the acquisition of a thing, by virtue of the realization of conditions, and it varies in intensity and weakness, and is called preparedness. An indication of preparedness possibility, which is the preparedness of matter for what it acquires of forms and accidents through the realization of some causes and conditions, such that it does not reach the limit of necessity occurring upon the completion of the cause, and it varies in intensity and weakness according to proximity to or distance from acquisition, based on the acquisition of much of what is indispensable or little, like the preparedness of humanity acquired by the sperm-drop, then the clot, then the chewed lump, and like the preparedness of writing acquired by the fetus, then the child, and so on until he learns. And this possibility is not necessary for the quiddity like essential possibility, but rather it exists after non-existence through the occurrence of some causes and conditions, and it ceases after existence through the actual acquisition of the thing.
Source pages p. 463

Commentary
This passage defines a second type of possibility: preparedness possibility (al-imkan al-isti'dadi). It is the preparedness of matter to receive forms and accidents when some conditions are realized, but it does not reach the level of necessity that occurs when the cause is complete. It varies in intensity and weakness depending on how close or far the acquisition is. Examples are given: the preparedness of a sperm-drop to become human, passing through stages, and the preparedness of a fetus to eventually learn writing. This possibility is not essential to the quiddity; rather, it arises after the thing's non-existence through the occurrence of some causes and conditions, and it ceases after the thing actually exists.

مادة
شرائط
شدة
